The Hadith on Fighting Until Shahada: The Boundary of Iman
Sanusi uses the hadith to prove that shahada marks the end of kufr and beginning of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that uttering the shahada marks the boundary between kufr and iman, and that sins such as withholding zakat do not negate this iman.

Core Idea
The shahada is the line: before it is kufr, after it is iman.

Sanusi presents a key hadith where the Prophet commands fighting until the shahada is uttered, indicating that shahada is the criterion for protection. He then uses the famous incident of Abu Bakr fighting those who withheld zakat to show that iman begins with the shahada, not with the performance of all obligations. This lesson establishes the understanding that iman is entered through the shahada, and that sins like neglecting zakat do not remove one from the fold of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the hadith is narrated with slight variations in wording.
2
Identify the boundary marker
Find the phrase that indicates when fighting stops and protection begins. The author explicitly states that the end of kufr is before the shahada and iman begins after it.
The key phrase is 'حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله'.
3
Understand the incident with Abu Bakr
Consider why Abu Bakr insisted on fighting those who withheld zakat. He argued that the shahada was the line, not the payment of zakat, but that zakat is a due that must be enforced.
This shows that sins do not negate the shahada.
4
Reflect on the multiple narrations
Notice that the hadith is recorded by several narrators with slight differences in wording. This confirms its authenticity and the lesson's importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف تقاتل الناس وقد قال رسول الله : أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله فمن قالها فقد عصم مني ماله ونفسه إلا بحقه وحسابه على الله فقال : والله لأقاتلن من فرّق بين الصلاة والزكاة فإن الزكاة حق المال والله لو منعوني عناقاً كانوا يؤدونها إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لقاتلتهم على منعها. قال عمر ـ رضي الله تعالى عنه - : فوالله ما هو إلا أن قد شرح الله صدر أبي بكر - رضي الله تعالى عنه ـ فعرفت أنه الحق. فتكون نهاية مرحلة الكفر هي ما قبل الشهادتين وما بعدها يكون بداية مرحلة الإيمان ورواه الترمذي والنسائي في السنن الكبرى عن أبي هريرة. وفي السنن الكبرى للنسائي رواه عن الزهري عن أنس بن مالك قال : " لما توفي رسول الله ا ارتدت العرب فقال عمر : يا أبا بكر كيف تقاتل العرب، فقال أبو بكر : إنما قال رسول الله : أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يشهدوا أن لا إله إلا الله وأني رسول الله ويقيموا الصلاة ويؤتوا الزكاة والله لو منعوني عناقاً مما كانوا يعطون رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لقاتلتهم علية قال عمر : فلما رأيت رأي أبي بكر قد شرح علمت أنه الحق. ورواه الطبراني في المعجم الكبير عن معاذ بن جبل بلفظ حتى يشهدوا ، وكذلك رواه البيهقي في السنن الكبرى، وعن أبي هريرة بلفظ يقولوا. وهذا الحديث محفوظ في كتب السنن (س).
"How can you fight the people when the Messenger of God said: I have been commanded to fight people until they say 'There is no god but God', so whoever says it has protected his wealth and life from me except for its due, and his reckoning is with God?" He said: "By God, I will fight those who separate prayer and zakat, for zakat is the due of wealth. By God, if they withhold from me a young she-goat that they used to give to the Messenger of God, I will fight them for withholding it." 'Umar said: "By God, it was not but that God had opened the heart of Abu Bakr, and I knew it was the truth." Thus, the end of the stage of kufr is before the two shahadas, and after them is the beginning of the stage of iman. And it was narrated by al-Tirmidhi and al-Nasa'i in al-Sunan al-Kubra from Abu Hurayra. And in al-Sunan al-Kubra of al-Nasa'i, it was narrated from al-Zuhri from Anas ibn Malik who said: "When the Messenger of God died, the Arabs apostatized. 'Umar said: O Abu Bakr, how can you fight the Arabs? Abu Bakr said: The Messenger of God said: I have been commanded to fight people until they testify that there is no god but God and that I am the Messenger of God, and establish prayer, and give zakat. By God, if they withhold from me a young she-goat that they used to give to the Messenger of God, I will fight them for it. 'Umar said: When I saw Abu Bakr's opinion had been opened up, I knew it was the truth." And it was narrated by al-Tabarani in al-Mu'jam al-Kabir from Mu'adh ibn Jabal with the wording "until they testify," and likewise by al-Bayhaqi in al-Sunan al-Kubra, and from Abu Hurayra with the wording "until they say." And this hadith is preserved in the Sunan books.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a prophetic hadith used to delineate the boundary between kufr and iman. The core argument is that the Prophet was commanded to fight people until they utter the shahada (testimony of faith). Once uttered, their life and property are protected. The author then cites the famous incident of Abu Bakr's decision to fight those who refused to pay zakat after the Prophet's death, arguing that refusal to pay zakat does not negate the shahada; rather, it is a separate due. The conclusion drawn is that the state of kufr ends precisely before the shahada, and iman begins after it. Multiple narrations confirm this teaching.

Key Terms
شهادة
إيمان
كفر
زكاة
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin after uttering the shahada can return a person to kufr.
Correction: This hadith shows that even refusing to pay zakat, a major sin, does not negate the iman established by the shahada. The shahada is the boundary; sins do not remove one from iman.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith and the author's conclusion, what marks the end of the stage of kufr?
A) Performing the five daily prayers
B) Paying zakat
C) Uttering the two shahadas
D) Belief in the heart without utterance

Key Takeaway
The shahada is the definitive boundary: uttering it ends kufr and begins iman; sins after it do not reverse this status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say the shahada, do you truly believe it is the dividing line between your past life of heedlessness and your present life of faith? What change does that require from you today?
?
Umar initially opposed Abu Bakr, but then his heart was opened and he admitted he was wrong. Is there a teaching of Islam you have resisted that you need to accept? Will you seek Allah to open your heart this week?
?
The hadith leaves final judgment to Allah: 'his reckoning is with God.' Do you secretly judge others' faith based on their outward obedience? This week, can you entrust their account to Allah and focus on your own?

Prophethood

Attributes of Prophets: Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
Sanusi applies the three rational judgments to prophets and lists their specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments regarding prophets and the specific attributes that are obligatory, impossible, and permissible for them.

Core Idea
Prophets are defined by what they must have, what they cannot have, and what they may have.

The same three categories of rational judgment—obligation, impossibility, and permissibility—that apply to Allah also apply to prophets. But the specific attributes differ. This passage from Sanusi's text enumerates what is wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for the messengers and prophets, establishing the boundaries of prophetic perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here, listing attributes in groups.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three rational judgments: obligation (wujub), impossibility (istihalah), and permissibility (jawaz). Find where each is mentioned and note the attributes listed under each.
The categories are the same as for Allah, but the attributes are specific to prophets.
3
Reflect on the attributes
Think about why each attribute is placed where it is. For example, why is truthfulness obligatory and falsehood impossible? How do permissible human attributes not diminish their rank?
This connects to the core idea: prophetic perfection is defined by these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمه شرعاً أن يعرف مثل الأقسام المتقدمة والتي هي الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز في حق الرسل ومثلهم الأنبياء، لأن كل رسول نبي، ويجب لهم الصدق والأمانة والفطانة، ويجب للرسل إثبات التبليغ ويستحيل في حقهم الكذب والخيانة والكتمان والغفلة، والبله، ويجوز في حقهم الأعراض البشرية التي لا تؤدي إلى النقص في مراتبهم العلية.
And it is legally required of him to know like the preceding divisions, which are obligation (wujub), impossibility (istihalah), and permissibility (jawaz) with regard to the messengers and similarly the prophets, because every messenger is a prophet. And they must have truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amanah), and intelligence (fatanah). And for the messengers, establishment of delivery (tabligh) is obligatory, and in their regard falsehood (kidhb), betrayal (khiyanah), concealment (kitman), heedlessness (ghaflah), and dullness (balah) are impossible. And permissible in their regard are human characteristics (a'rad bashariyya) that do not lead to deficiency in their lofty stations.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage outlines the obligations concerning belief in prophets. It states that it is legally required (yulzimuhu shar'an) for a person to know the three categories of judgment—obligatory (wujub), impossible (istihalah), and permissible (jawaz)—as applied to messengers (rusul) and prophets (anbiya'), since every messenger is a prophet. The specific obligatory attributes for them are: truthfulness (sidq) in conveying revelation, trustworthiness (amanah) in guarding the message, and intelligence (fatanah) to understand and communicate. Additionally, for messengers specifically, delivery of the message (tabligh) is obligatory. Conversely, the following are impossible for them: lying (kidhb), betrayal (khiyanah) of the trust, concealment (kitman) of any part of the message, heedlessness (ghaflah) in delivering it, and dullness (balah) of intellect. Finally, human experiences (a'rad bashariyya) that do not diminish their high status are permissible for them, illustrating their humanity while protecting their prophetic dignity.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
الصدق
الأمانة
الفطانة
التبليغ
الأعراض البشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think prophets can commit minor sins or be forgetful in delivering the message.
Correction: This passage clearly states that lying, betrayal, concealment, heedlessness, and dullness are impossible for prophets, preserving their complete trustworthiness and intelligence.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered obligatory for messengers according to this passage?
A) Concealment
B) Delivery of the message
C) Human weaknesses
D) Heedlessness

Key Takeaway
Prophets are characterized by necessary perfections, impossible imperfections, and permissible human traits that do not diminish their status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the attribute of 'trustworthiness' (amanah) that is wajib for every prophet. In your daily life, where are you trusted by others? Are you living up to that trust as a reflection of prophetic character, or are there areas where you betray trust through small deceptions? What specific step can you take today to be more trustworthy?
?
The passage teaches that 'heedlessness' (ghafla) is impossible for prophets. But you and I are not protected from heedlessness. When was the last time you felt spiritually heedless—forgetful of Allah or your purpose? What practices from the prophetic model (such as regular dhikr or prayer) can you adopt to combat your own tendency toward ghafla?
?
Prophets have human traits like hunger and fatigue that are ja'iz (permissible). Does this closeness of the prophets to your own human experiences make it easier or harder to follow their example? How can you use a shared human need—like a meal or sleep—as an opportunity to connect with the prophetic way (sunnah) and draw nearer to Allah?

The Istisqa Hadith and the Name al-Ghani
Long hadith of prayer for rain cited, followed by a note confirming the name al-Ghani is frequent in revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the istisqa hadith serves as evidence for the divine name al-Ghani and that this name is abundantly confirmed in the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The istisqa hadith is one of many proofs that Allah is al-Ghani, the Self-Sufficient.

In this passage, the author presents a famous hadith of the Prophet's prayer for rain. The central invocation—'O God, You are al-Ghani and we are the needy'—anchors the divine attribute of self-sufficiency in prophetic practice. The author then notes that this name appears frequently in revelation, not just here.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage fully, noting the prophetic actions and especially the supplication.
Focus on the phrase 'الغني ونحن الفقراء'.
2
Identify the evidence for al-Ghani
The hadith explicitly calls Allah al-Ghani. Notice how the author uses this as textual evidence.
3
Understand the authenticity discussion
The author states the hadith is authentic by Bukhari and Muslim's standards but they didn't record it, and then lists other narrators.
This is a scholarly note on reliability.
4
Connect to the broader theme
The concluding sentence affirms that al-Ghani is frequent in Quran and Sunnah. This counters the misconception that this single hadith is the only proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكبر وحمد الله ثم قال إنكم شكوتم جدب دياركم واستئخار المطر عن أوان زمانه وقد أمركم الله أن تدعوه ووعدكم أن يستجيب لكم ثم قال الحمد لله رب العالمين الرحمن الرحيم مالك يوم الدين لا إله إلا الله يفعل ما يريد اللهم أنت لا إله إلا أنت الغني ونحن الفقراء أنزل علينا الغيث واجعل ما أنزلت لنا قوة وبلاغاً إلى حين ثم رفع يديه فلم يزل في الرفع حتى بدا بياض إبطيه ثم حول إلى الناس ظهره وقلب أو حول رداءه وهو رافع يديه ثم أقبل على الناس ونزل فصلى ركعتين فأنشأ الله سحاباً فرعدت وبرقت ثم أمطرت بإذن الله فلم يأت مسجده حتى سالت السيول فلما رأى سرعتهم إلى الكن ضحك حتى بدت نواجذه فقال أشهد أن الله على كل شيء قدير وأني عبد الله ورسوله. هذا حديث صحيح على شرط الشيخين ولم يخرجاه. ورواه أبو داود في سننه عن عائشة - رضي الله تعالى عنها ، وفيه اللهم أنت الله لا إله إلا أنت الغني ونحن الفقراء الخ . وبهذا اللفظ أيضاً رواه البيهقي في الكبرى عن عائشة أيضاً، وابن حبان في صحيحه. وأما اسم الغني لله تعالى فهو وارد في القرآن وفي السنة كثيراً في الأحاديث التي فيها عد الأسماء الحسنى.
He said Allahu Akbar and praised God, then said: 'You complained of the drought of your lands and the delay of rain from its time, and God has commanded you to supplicate Him and promised to respond. Then he said: Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, the Compassionate, the Merciful, Master of the Day of Judgment. There is no deity but God, He does what He wills. O God, You are the only deity, You are the Self-Sufficient, and we are the needy. Send down rain upon us and make what You send down strength and provision for a time.' Then he raised his hands until the whiteness of his armpits appeared. Then he turned his back to the people and turned his cloak, while raising his hands. Then he faced the people and came down and prayed two rak'ahs, and God created clouds that thundered and lightninged, then it rained by God's permission. Before he returned to his mosque, the torrents flowed. When he saw their eagerness for the shelter, he laughed until his molars appeared and said: 'I testify that God is powerful over everything and that I am the servant and messenger of God.' This hadith is authentic according to the conditions of al-Bukhari and Muslim, but they did not record it. Abu Dawud narrated it in his Sunan from 'A'isha, and in it: 'O God, You are God, there is no deity but You, the Self-Sufficient, and we are the needy' etc. Also, al-Bayhaqi in his Sunan al-Kubra from 'A'isha and Ibn Hibban in his Sahih narrated it with this wording. As for the name al-Ghani for God, it occurs frequently in the Quran and in the Sunnah in the hadiths that list the Most Beautiful Names.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known hadith of the Prophet Muhammad performing the prayer for rain (istisqa). It begins with the Prophet's sermon acknowledging the drought and urging supplication. The key phrase for the lesson is 'O God, You are the only deity, You are the Self-Sufficient (al-Ghani), and we are the needy.' The hadith then describes the raising of hands, turning his cloak (a sunna), and the subsequent rain. The author then discusses the authenticity: it meets the criteria of Bukhari and Muslim but they did not include it; it is narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn Hibban. Finally, the author notes that the name al-Ghani is abundantly present in both the Quran and the Sunnah, especially in hadiths enumerating the Beautiful Names. This section reinforces the earlier evidence for God's attribute of ghina (self-sufficiency) and serves as a practical example of the name's usage in prophetic prayer.

Key Terms
الاستسقاء
الغني
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This hadith is the only source for the name al-Ghani.
Correction: The author states that the name al-Ghani occurs frequently in the Quran and Sunnah, especially in hadiths listing the Most Beautiful Names.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of citing this long hadith in the context of divine attributes?
A) To describe the method of praying for rain.
B) To provide textual evidence for the name al-Ghani from the Sunnah.
C) To prove God answers prayers.
D) To show the Prophet's humility.

Key Takeaway
The istisqa hadith provides a clear prophetic invocation of the name al-Ghani, and the author confirms that this name is abundantly established in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you feel the weight of your poverty before Allah as the Prophet did, or do you approach Him casually? Pause and ask yourself: what one area of your life have you been acting as if you don’t need Him? Commit to making a sincere du'a today acknowledging your need in that area.
?
If Allah is completely self-sufficient, then why does He command you to ask Him? Let that reality sink in: your supplication is not for His benefit but for yours. Are you neglecting this gift? Resolve to make du'a a daily habit, not just in emergencies.
?
The Prophet laughed when the rain came quickly, and testified to Allah's power. Think of a time Allah answered your prayer swiftly. Did you respond with gratitude and testimony, or did you take it for granted? Make a list of three answered prayers you can thank Him for today.

Prophethood

Prophethood as a Divine Favor: Included in the Permissible
Foudah refutes four erroneous positions on prophethood and affirms it as a divine favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophethood is classified as permissible (ja'iz) for Allah and how this refutes various sectarian errors.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a pure favor from Allah, not obligatory, impossible, or acquired.

This note by Said Foudah clarifies a critical point in kalam: where does prophethood fall among the three rational judgments? After establishing that everything that is not obligatory or impossible is permissible for Allah, we now see that prophethood is exactly that—a permissible act of divine favor. The text then refutes four deviant positions that either overstate or understate God's freedom in sending messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic note and translation. Notice the four refuted positions and the author's judgment on each.
2
Identify the four deviant views
List each sect and their claim about prophethood. See how each is excluded from the Sunni position.
3
Understand the Sunni position
The passage concludes that prophethood is a favor (fadl) from Allah. Ask: Why is this important for theology?
4
Reflect on the labels
Note that the author calls some groups 'astray' (ضلوا) and others 'innovators' (مبتدعة). What is the difference?
5
Connect to the broader framework
This lesson shows how the category of ja'iz applies to a specific doctrine. See how kalam uses rational categories to defend orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة: دخل في الجائز بعثة الرسل فليست بواجبة كما قالت المعتزلة ولا مستحيلة كما قالت البراهمة والسَّمَنِيّة، ولا مكتسبة كما قالت الفلاسفة ولا ذاتية كما قالت الكرَّامية، فالبراهمة والفلاسفة ضلوا والمعتزلة والكرامية مبتدعة. ومذهب أهل السنة أن النبوة والرسالة فضل من الله تعالى.
Note: Included in the permissible is the sending of messengers, so it is not obligatory as the Mu'tazila said, nor impossible as the Brahmins and Samaniyya said, nor acquired as the philosophers said, nor essential as the Karramiyya said. So the Brahmins and philosophers have gone astray, and the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya are innovators. The doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that prophethood and messengership are a favor from God, may He be Exalted.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a note (ملاحظة) that clarifies that prophethood falls under the category of what is permissible for God. It refutes several deviant positions: the Mu'tazila who say prophethood is obligatory on God, the Brahmins and Samaniyya who say it is impossible, the philosophers who say it is acquired (i.e., a human achievement), and the Karramiyya who say it is an essential attribute of God. The author labels the former two as astray and the latter two as innovators. The correct Sunni view is that prophethood is a pure favor (فضل) from God, not obligatory on Him.

Key Terms
بعثة الرسل
الفضل
المعتزلة
البراهمة
الفلاسفة
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophethood is necessary for God's justice or that it is impossible for God to send messengers.
Correction: The passage clarifies that prophethood is not obligatory (contra Mu'tazila) nor impossible (contra Brahmins); it is a permissible act of divine favor.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which sect claimed that prophethood is essential (ذاتية) for God?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Brahmins
C) Philosophers
D) Karramiyya

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is not necessary or impossible for Allah but is a gratuitous favor from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken Allah's guidance for granted, as if He owed it to you? This lesson says prophethood is a pure favor. How does that realization change the way you receive and respond to divine guidance today?
?
When you read about the sects that went astray—some demanding prophethood as obligatory, others calling it impossible—do you see any tendency in your own heart to dictate terms to Allah? Will you repent from that attitude and embrace His favor with humility?
?
If prophethood is a favor, not a necessity, then every prophet's message becomes an unearned gift. How will you treat the gift of prophetic teachings (including this lesson) as a favor that demands gratitude, not casual indifference?

Defining the Messenger (Rasul) and Its Exclusions
Sanusi defines rasul and lists excluded categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a messenger (rasul) in Islamic theology and the categories excluded from it, including the distinction between a rasul and a nabi.

Core Idea
A messenger must be a free, male, adult, intelligent human commanded to convey revelation.

Sanusi provides a precise technical definition of the term 'rasul' by listing its necessary conditions. Each condition automatically excludes certain groups, clarifying who can and cannot be a messenger. This definition also sets apart the prophet (nabi) from the messenger, a key distinction in prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its conditions
Start by reading the Arabic definition of rasul. Notice the seven conditions: human, free, male, adult, intelligent, receives revelation of a law, and is commanded to convey it.
The definition is compact; each word carries theological weight.
2
Identify the exclusions
Go through each exclusion mentioned: angels, jinn, slaves, females/hermaphrodites/children, the mentally unfit, false prophets, and finally prophets. See how each exclusion corresponds to a condition.
The exclusion of prophets is the most subtle—they lack the command to convey.
3
Distinguish rasul and nabi
Compare the definition of rasul with that of nabi. The only difference is the command to convey. That means every rasul is a nabi, but not every nabi is a rasul.
4
Reflect on the purpose of exclusions
Consider why each condition is necessary. For example, why must a messenger be human? Why free? This clarifies the nature of prophethood and the role of messengers.
These exclusions guard against misconceptions about who can be a prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرسول (١) هو إنسان حر ذكر بالغ فطن أوحى الله إليه بشرع وأمره بتبليغه للعباد سواء كان له كتاب أم لا. فخرج الملائكة والجن فإن الله تعالى لم يرسل إلينا ملكا ولا جنياً، وخرج العبد لأن الرسول لا يكون إلا حراً، وخرج الأنثى والخنثى والصبي، وخرج الأبله والبليد والمتنبئ وهو من يدعي النبوة وهو كذاب، وخرج أيضاً من هذا التعريف النبي قال "أمره بتبليغه" فالنبي هو إنسان حر بالغ فطن أوحى الله إليه بشرع وإن لم يؤمر بتبليغه
The messenger (rasul) is a free, male, adult, intelligent human being to whom God revealed a law and commanded him to convey it to the servants, whether he has a book or not. Thus excluded are the angels and the jinn, for God did not send to us an angel or a jinni. Excluded is the slave, because the messenger can only be free. Excluded is the female, the hermaphrodite, and the child. Excluded is the imbecile, the dull-witted, and the false prophet (mutanabbi'), who is one who claims prophethood and is a liar. Also excluded from this definition is the prophet (nabi), because the definition says 'commanded him to convey it', so the prophet is a free, male, adult, intelligent human being to whom God revealed a law, even if he was not commanded to convey it.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'rasul' (messenger) and lists its conditions and exclusions. The definition requires the messenger to be a human, free, male, adult, intelligent, receiving revelation of a law, and commanded to convey it. Each condition excludes certain categories: angels and jinn (not human), slaves (not free), females and hermaphrodites (not male), children (not adult), the mentally unfit (not intelligent), false prophets (not truthful). Finally, the prophet (nabi) is distinguished: he shares all conditions except the command to convey, so he is not necessarily a messenger.

Key Terms
رسول
نبي
متنبئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that prophets (anbiya') are never commanded to convey revelation, and that rasul and nabi are completely separate.
Correction: The passage shows that the only difference is the command to convey. Prophets may still convey if they choose, but they are not obligated like a messenger. Moreover, every messenger is a prophet, so they overlap.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is excluded from the definition of rasul because they are not human?
A) Slaves
B) Angels and jinn
C) False prophets
D) Prophets

Key Takeaway
A messenger is a human, free, male, adult, intelligent being who receives revelation and is commanded to convey it, while a prophet shares all but the command to convey.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson defines a messenger as free, male, adult, intelligent. Does your heart accept that Allah's wisdom chose these specific criteria, or do you find yourself questioning His choice? How does this affect your trust in His decisions?
?
If messengers are only human, and you believe Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the last, then you are left with the command to convey. What message have you actively conveyed today? When was the last time you spoke about Islam to someone who needed to hear it?
?
The false prophet (mutanabbi') is excluded because he claims prophethood falsely. Are there any areas in your life where you claim religious authority you do not have, or speak about Allah without knowledge? Repent and commit to only speaking with evidence.

Scholarly Opinions on the Difference Between Nabi and Rasul
Presents various scholarly views on nabi vs. rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly views on whether the terms nabi (prophet) and rasul (messenger) are synonymous or distinct, and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree whether nabi and rasul are synonymous or distinct.

In this lesson, we explore a passage that presents multiple classical opinions on the relationship between the terms 'nabi' and 'rasul'. While some scholars like al-Taftazani and al-Sanusi see them as synonyms, the majority, including al-Baghdadi and al-Baydawi, distinguish them based on the nature of revelation and law. Understanding these differences helps clarify the broader concept of prophethood in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to each scholar's name and their stated position.
The passage is dense with opinions; read slowly.
2
Identify each scholar's opinion
List the scholars mentioned: al-Baghdadi, al-Taftazani, al-Sanusi, Mulla Ahmad al-Jindi, and al-Qadi al-Baydawi. For each, note whether they consider nabi and rasul synonymous or distinct.
Al-Baghdadi gives a distinction; al-Taftazani leans toward synonymy; al-Sanusi follows al-Taftazani; Mulla Ahmad notes the majority oppose synonymy; al-Baydawi provides multiple distinctions.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
For each opinion, understand the basis: al-Baghdadi focuses on the nature of the message (new or abrogating law), al-Taftazani defines both as sent with revelation, al-Baydawi offers multiple criteria (new law, book, mode of revelation).
Al-Baydawi's comments are based on Quran 22:52.
4
Consider why this distinction matters
Reflect on the theological implications: if they are synonymous, the category of prophethood is undifferentiated; if distinct, it affects our understanding of the roles of prophets and messengers in conveying divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام عبد القاهر البغدادي: والفرق بينهما أي بين النبي والرسول - أن النبي من أتاه الوحي عز وجل ونزل عليه الملك بالوحي والرسول من يأتي بشرع على الابتداء أو بنسخ بعض أحكام شريعة قبله أهـ، ومال الإمام التفتازاني كما في شرح المقاصد إلى الترادف بين معنى النبي والرسول ، فقال : النبي إنسان بعثه الله لتبليغ ما أوحى إليه ، وكذا الرسول أهـ. وعلى هذا مشى الإمام السنوسي في أم البراهين. وقال الملا أحمد الجندي في حاشيته على شرح العقائد : لكنه خلاف ما عليه الجمهور، وما اختاره القاضي البيضاوي في تفسير قوله تعالى ( وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن قَبْلِكَ مِن رَّسُولٍ وَلَا نَبِي ) الآية، حيث قال الرسول من بعثه الله بشريعة متجددة يدعو الناس إليها والنبي يعمه ومن بعثه إلى الخلق لتقرير شرع سابق. وقيل الرسول من جمع إلى المعجزة كتاباً منزلاً عليه والنبي غير الرسول من لا كتاب له. وقيل الرسول من يأتيه الملك بالوحي والنبي يقال لمن يوحى إليه في المنام أهـ كلام القاضي قال الملا أحمد ولم يتعرض للمساواة أهـ كلامه، ففهم أن قوله أن اختيار السعد خلاف الجمهور من حيث القول بالمساواة.
Imam 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi said: The difference between them, i.e., between the prophet (nabi) and the messenger (rasul), is that the prophet is one to whom revelation comes, may He be exalted, and the angel descends to him with revelation, while the messenger is one who brings a law anew or abrogates some rulings of a law before him. End quote. Imam al-Taftazani, as in Sharh al-Maqasid, inclined toward synonymy between the meanings of prophet and messenger, and said: The prophet is a human being whom God sent to convey what was revealed to him, and likewise the messenger. End quote. Imam al-Sanusi followed this in Umm al-Barahin. Mulla Ahmad al-Jindi said in his gloss on Sharh al-'Aqa'id: But it is contrary to what the majority (jumhur) hold, and what al-Qadi al-Baydawi chose in his interpretation of His statement (and We did not send before you any messenger or prophet) [Qur'an 22:52], where he said: The messenger is one whom God sent with a renewed law, calling people to it; the prophet is more general and includes one sent to creation to confirm a previous law. And it was said: The messenger is one who combines miracle with a revealed book; the prophet who is not a messenger is one without a book. And it was said: The messenger is one to whom the angel comes with revelation; the prophet is said of one to whom revelation comes in a dream. End quote from al-Qadi. Mulla Ahmad said: He did not address synonymy. End quote. So one understands that his statement that al-Sa'd's choice is contrary to the majority is with respect to the claim of synonymy.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This footnote presents several scholarly views on the difference between prophet (nabi) and messenger (rasul). Imam al-Baghdadi holds that a prophet receives revelation while a messenger brings a new or abrogating law. Imam al-Taftazani leans toward synonymy, followed by Imam al-Sanusi. Mulla Ahmad al-Jindi notes that the majority oppose synonymy and cites al-Baydawi's interpretation of Qur'an 22:52, which distinguishes them. Al-Baydawi offers multiple opinions: one says a messenger brings a new law, a prophet may merely confirm previous law; another says a messenger has a revealed book; another says a messenger receives revelation through an angel while a prophet may receive through dreams. The author then comments that al-Sanusi's choice is against the majority.

Key Terms
نبي
رسول
الترادف
الفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that nabi and rasul are distinct categories.
Correction: Some major scholars like al-Taftazani and al-Sanusi held that they are synonymous, while the majority distinguished them.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Baghdadi, what is the main difference between a prophet and a messenger?
A) A messenger has a book
B) A messenger brings a new or abrogating law
C) A prophet cannot convey revelation
D) They are the same

Key Takeaway
The relationship between nabi and rasul is a matter of scholarly debate; while some regard them as synonymous, the majority distinguish them based on the nature of revelation and law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a strong intuition or 'inspiration' to act, do you automatically assume it is from God? How does this lesson's distinction between angelic revelation and dream revelation challenge you to be more discerning about the source of your inspirations?
?
Are you more comfortable with clear-cut definitions or with scholarly disagreement? How does your desire for certainty affect your trust in God's wisdom in allowing multiple valid views?
?
If you were to live as a 'prophet' in the general sense of calling others to God, what 'law' (sharia) are you confirming? Examine your life: are you truly confirming the revealed law, or are you adding your own preferences?

The Relied-Upon Distinction Between Prophet and Messenger
The author presents the relied-upon view on the distinction between prophet and messenger, and supports it with quotations from al-Dasuqi and al-Halimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relied-upon distinction between a prophet (nabi) and a messenger (rasul) in Islamic theology, including the definitions and supporting evidence from al-Dasuqi and al-Halimi.

Core Idea
A prophet receives revelation; a messenger is commanded to convey it.

This lesson clarifies the precise theological distinction between a prophet and a messenger. While both receive divine revelation, the key difference lies in the command to convey that revelation to others. We will explore the relied-upon view and examine supporting statements from two major scholars, al-Dasuqi and al-Halimi.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the definitions of nabi and rasul as given in the passage. Note that a prophet is more general than a messenger.
The relied-upon view does not require a messenger to have a book.
2
Examine the opposing views
Consider the two alternative views that require a book or an abrogating law. Understand why these are criticized.
The first view is objected because there are many messengers but few books.
3
Analyze al-Halimi's explanation
Break down al-Halimi's derivation of prophethood from 'news' and his condition for messengerhood: the command to convey.
Al-Halimi emphasizes that a prophet may be private or public.
4
Synthesize the distinction
Conclude that every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.
This is the core of the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النبي فهو إنسان أوحى إليه بشرع أمر بتبليغة أم لا. فالنبي أعم من الرسول مطلقاً، هذا هو المعتمد، ومقابله قولان : الأول أن الرسول إنسان أوحي إليه بشرع وكان له كتاب، فلا بد في الرسول من الكتاب والشريعة ولا يلزم من كونه له كتاب أن يكون له شريعة لاحتمال أن يكون ما في الكتاب مواعظ، واعترض هذا القول بأن الكتب قليلة والرسل كثيرة فكيف يشترط في الرسول أن يكون له كتاب والقول الثاني يقول لا بد في الرسول من أحد أمرين إما أن يكون له كتاب وإما أن تكون شریعته ناسخة لشريعة من قبله، فإذا نزلت التوراة على موسى وأوحي إلى نبي من بني إسرائيل مثلاً بتبليغ أحكامها ولم ينزل عليه كتاب ولم تكن شريعته ناسخة لشريعة موسى فلا يكون رسولاً .انتهى من حاشية العلامة المحقق الدسوقي على شرح أم البراهين للسنوسي. وهي من أعظم الحواشي وأكثرها فوائد. وقد قال الإمام الحليمي في المنهاج في معنى النبوة والفرق بين النبي والرسول: "إن النبوة اسم مشتق من النبأ وهو الخبر، إلا أن المراد به في هذا الموضع خبر خاص وهو الذي يلزم الله عز وجل به أحداً من عباده فيميزه بإلقائه إليه عن غيره، ويوقفه به على شريعته بما فيها من أمر ونهي ووعظ وإرشاد ووعد ووعيد، فتكون النبوة على هذا : الخبر والمعرفة بالمخبرات الموصوفة التي ذكرتها ، والنبي هو المخبر بها. فإن انضاف إلى هذا التوفيق أمر تبليغه إلى الناس ودعائهم إليه ، كان نبياً رسولاً. وإن ألقي إليه ليعمل به في خاصته ولم يؤمر بتبليغه والدعاء إليه كان نبياً ولم يكن رسولاً ، فكل رسول نبي، وليس كل نبي رسولاً ". أهـ. (س)
As for the prophet (nabi), he is a human being to whom a law was revealed, whether commanded to convey it or not. So the prophet is more general than the messenger absolutely; this is the relied-upon view. Opposing it are two views: The first is that the messenger is a human being to whom a law was revealed and he had a book, so a messenger must have a book and a law, but having a book does not entail having a law because it is possible that what is in the book is admonitions. This view was objected to on the grounds that books are few and messengers are many, so how can it be required that a messenger have a book? The second view says that a messenger must have one of two things: either he has a book or his law abrogates the law of his predecessor. So if the Torah was revealed to Moses and revelation came to a prophet of the Children of Israel, for example, to convey its rulings, and no book was revealed to him and his law did not abrogate the law of Moses, then he is not a messenger. End quote from the gloss of the verifying scholar al-Dasuqi on Sharh Umm al-Barahin of al-Sanusi. It is among the greatest glosses and most beneficial. Imam al-Halimi said in al-Minhaj concerning the meaning of prophethood and the difference between the prophet and the messenger: 'Prophethood is a name derived from naba' (news), except that what is intended in this context is a specific news, which is that which God, may He be glorified and exalted, imposes upon one of His servants, distinguishing him by casting it to him from others, and making him aware through it of His law including its command, prohibition, admonition, guidance, promise, and threat. So prophethood on this basis is the news and knowledge of the described reports that I have mentioned, and the prophet is the one who reports them. If to this success is added the command to convey it to the people and call them to it, then he is a prophet and a messenger. If it is cast to him to act upon it privately and he is not commanded to convey it and call to it, then he is a prophet and not a messenger. So every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.' End quote. (S)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage continues the footnote from the previous page. It presents the relied-upon definition: a prophet (nabi) is a human who receives revelation whether commanded to convey or not, making the term 'prophet' more general than 'messenger'. Two opposing views are mentioned: one requires a messenger to have a book; the other requires either a book or an abrogating law. Both are criticized. Then a quote from Imam al-Halimi explains that prophethood is derived from 'news', and that if one is commanded to convey, he is a messenger; otherwise, a prophet. Thus, every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

Key Terms
نبي
رسول
المعتمد
ناسخة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a prophet never conveys revelation to others.
Correction: The relied-upon view states that a prophet may or may not be commanded to convey; if commanded, he is also a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Halimi, what distinguishes a messenger from a prophet?
A) A messenger has a revealed book
B) A messenger is commanded to convey to people
C) A messenger performs miracles
D) A messenger is always free

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view is that a prophet is a human receiving revelation, while a messenger is a prophet commanded to convey it; every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that every messenger is a prophet but not every prophet is a messenger. Consider the heavy responsibility of conveying Allah's message. Are you actively conveying the truth you know to others, even if only to your family and friends? If not, what is holding you back?
?
The passage mentions scholars holding different views on this distinction. Do you approach controversial theological topics with a sincere desire for truth, or do you cling to opinions out of pride or tradition? How can you cultivate humility in seeking knowledge?
?
Prophets received revelation directly from Allah. The Quran is now in your hands. How often do you read it with the reverence it deserves, knowing it is the very word of the One who sent the prophets? What can you change in your daily routine to honor this gift?

Defending the Scholarly Distinction Between Prophet and Messenger
The author defends the scholarly definition against hasty rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's defense of the scholarly distinction between prophethood and messengership and why hasty rejection reflects a lack of appreciation for gradation in prophetic ranks.

Core Idea
Not all criticism is scholarly; hasty rejection ignores the gradation of prophethood.

In this passage, the author responds to those who dismiss the scholarly definition of prophet and messenger as baseless. He argues that such categorical rejection is contrary to the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna, which allows for nuance and possibility. He then sets the stage for an explanation of the gradation in the Prophetic station of Muhammad (PBUH).

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the author's tone
Read the Arabic passage once and notice the author's defensive and critical tone. He is addressing a specific objection.
The author is surprised by the source of the objection.
2
Understand the critique of hasty judgment
Pay attention to the author's criticism of those who categorically declared the scholars mistaken. He accuses them of blind imitation and contradiction of the Qur'an.
3
Recognize the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna
The author notes that Ahl al-Sunna does not make categorical judgments where there is possibility. This is a key methodological principle.
Contrast this with the approach of the anthropomorphists (mujassima).
4
Grasp the concept of gradation (tadarruj)
The author promises to explain how scholars understood the ascending ranks of prophethood and messengership in Prophet Muhammad. This gradation is central to their definition.
5
Prepare for the upcoming explanation
The lesson sets the stage for a detailed treatment elsewhere. This passage is a prelude to a more focused discussion.
Keep in mind that the author will elaborate on the gradation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الحقيقة لا بد هنا من أن نوضح زيادة توضيح الفرق بين النبوة والرسالة، ونوجه قول علمائنا الذين مشوا على هذا التعريف، خلافاً لمن تسرّع وجزم بغلطهم في هذا التعريف، أي في تعريف النبي والرسول، وأوحى إلى القراء أن قولهم ليس بمستند إلى حجة مطلقاً بل هو في غايته مبالغة في التقليد وخطأ محض ومخالفة لصريح القرآن ؟ ! مع أنه ليس من شأن أهل السنة الجزم بالأمر ما دام فيه بعض الاحتمال ! ولو أن هذا الاعتراض صدر من قبل المخالفين من المجسمة لما استدعى هذا أن نفصل في المسألة هنا، ولكن صدر هذا القول ممن ينتسب إلى أهل السنة، فاقتضى العجب. وسوف نذكر كلاماً مختصراً هنا لأنه لا يليق التفصيل، نبين ونوجه فيه التعريف المذكور أعلاه ، وللتفصيل محل آخر. فنقول وبالله التوفيق : سنبين فيما يلي كيف فهم العلماء تدرج سيدنا محمد في مراتب النبوة والرسالة، ليتبين للقارئ الكريم ما الذي يريدونه بالفرق بين النبي والرسول. ومن جهل هذا التدرج سهل عليه مخالفتهم في تفريقهم.
In truth, here we must provide additional clarification of the difference between prophethood (nubuwwa) and messengership (risala), and justify the statement of our scholars who followed this definition, contrary to those who rushed and categorically declared them mistaken in this definition, i.e., in the definition of the prophet and the messenger, and indicated to the readers that their statement is not based on any evidence at all, but rather it is extreme blind imitation, pure error, and contradiction of the clear Qur'an?! While it is not the way of Ahl al-Sunna to be categorical about a matter as long as there remains some possibility! And if this objection had come from opponents among the anthropomorphists (mujassima), it would not have required us to discuss the issue here, but this statement came from one who claims affiliation with Ahl al-Sunna, so it is astonishing. We will present a brief statement here, as detailed discussion is not appropriate, in which we will clarify and justify the aforementioned definition, while a full treatment belongs elsewhere. So we say, and with God is success: We will show below how the scholars understood the ascending ranks of our master Muhammad in the stations of prophethood and messengership, so that the noble reader may understand what they intend by the difference between the prophet and the messenger. Whoever is ignorant of this gradation will find it easy to oppose them in their distinction.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is the author's own commentary. He insists on the need to clarify the difference between prophethood (nubuwwa) and messengership (risala) and to justify the definition used by scholars. He criticizes those who rashly accuse scholars of error, calling it blind imitation and contradiction of the Qur'an. He notes that it is not the way of Ahl al-Sunna to be categorical when there is possibility. He expresses surprise that such criticism comes from those claiming to be Ahl al-Sunna rather than from opponents like the anthropomorphists (mujassima). He promises a brief explanation, noting that a fuller treatment belongs elsewhere, and then announces that he will show how scholars understood the ascending ranks of prophethood and messengership of Prophet Muhammad, to clarify what they mean by the distinction.

The Relied-Upon Definition of Prophet and Messenger
Sanusi presents the standard definition and quotes two alternative views from al-Dasuqi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard Ash'ari definition of prophet (nabi) and messenger (rasul) and the two alternative views regarding the requirement of a scripture.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

The relationship between prophethood and messengership is a foundational topic in Islamic theology. In this lesson, we explore the relied-upon definition and two minority views on what makes a messenger distinct from a prophet, as presented by Sanusi and glossed by al-Dasuqi.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first part of the passage where the standard definition is given. Notice the key phrase: 'فكل رسول نبي وليس كل نبي رسولاً'.
2
Understand the two alternative views
Two opposing views are cited from al-Dasuqi's gloss. The first requires a messenger to have a book, the second requires either a book or an abrogating law. Consider the objections raised.
3
Reflect on the relied-upon view
Why does the author prefer the standard definition? Think about its simplicity and scriptural basis.
This connects to the broader theological method of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للخلق (١) فالرسول أخص من النبي والنبي أعم فكل رسول نبي وليس كل نبي رسولاً فبعض النبي رسول وبعض النبي ليس برسول إذا لم يؤمر بالتبليغ (٢) (١) وأما النبي فهو إنسان أوحى إليه بشرع أمر بتبليغة أم لا فالنبي أعم من الرسول مطلقاً هذا هو المعتمد ومقابله قولان الأول أن الرسول إنسان أوحي إليه بشرع وكان له كتاب فلا بد في الرسول من الكتاب والشريعة ولا يلزم من كونه له كتاب أن يكون له شريعة لاحتمال أن يكون ما في الكتاب مواعظ واعترض هذا القول بأن الكتب قليلة والرسل كثيرة فكيف يشترط في الرسول أن يكون له كتاب والقول الثاني يقول لا بد في الرسول من أحد أمرين إما أن يكون له كتاب وإما أن تكون شريعته ناسخة لشريعة من قبله فإذا نزلت التوراة على موسى وأوحي إلى نبي من بني إسرائيل مثلاً بتبليغ أحكامها ولم ينزل عليه كتاب ولم تكن شريعته ناسخة لشريعة موسى فلا يكون رسولاً انتهى من حاشية العلامة المحقق الدسوقي على شرح أم البراهين للسنوسي وهي من أعظم الحواشي وأكثرها فوائد وقد قال الإمام الحليمي في المنهاج في معنى النبوة والفرق بين النبي والرسول إن النبوة اسم مشتق من النبأ وهو الخبر إلا أن المراد به في هذا الموضع خبر خاص وهو الذي يلزم الله عز وجل به أحداً من عباده فيميزه بإلقائه إليه عن غيره ويوقفه به على شريعته بما فيها من أمر ونهي ووعظ وإرشاد ووعد ووعيد فتكون النبوة على هذا الخبر والمعرفة بالمخبرات الموصوفة التي ذكرتها والنبي هو المخبر بها فإن انضاف إلى هذا التوفيق أمر تبليغه إلى الناس ودعائهم إليه كان نبياً رسولاً وإن ألقي إليه ليعمل به في خاصته ولم يؤمر بتبليغه والدعاء إليه كان نبياً ولم يكن رسولاً فكل رسول نبي وليس كل نبي رسولاً أهـ (س) (٢) في الحقيقة لا بد هنا من أن نوضح زيادة توضيح الفرق بين النبوة والرسالة ونوجه قول علمائنا الذين مشوا على هذا التعريف خلافاً لمن تسرع وجزم بغلطهم في هذا التعريف أي في تعريف النبي والرسول وأوحى إلى القراء أن قولهم ليس بمستند إلى حجة مطلقاً بل هو في غايته مبالغة في التقليد وخطأ محض ومخالفة لصريح القرآن مع أنه ليس من شأن أهل السنة الجزم بالأمر ما دام فيه بعض الاحتمال ولو أن هذا الاعتراض صدر من قبل المخالفين من المجسمة لما استدعى هذا أن نفصل في المسألة هنا ولكن صدر هذا القول ممن ينتسب إلى أهل السنة فاقتضى العجب وسوف نذكر كلاماً مختصراً هنا لأنه لا يليق التفصيل نبين ونوجه فيه التعريف المذكور أعلاه وللتفصيل محل آخر فنقول وبالله التوفيق سنبين فيما يلي كيف فهم العلماء تدرج سيدنا محمد في مراتب النبوة والرسالة ليتبين للقارئ الكريم ما الذي يريدونه بالفرق بين النبي والرسول ومن جهل هذا التدرج سهل عليه مخالفتهم في تفريقهم
For creation: the messenger (rasul) is more specific than the prophet (nabi), and the prophet is more general. So every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. Some prophets are messengers and some prophets are not messengers if they are not commanded to convey. (1) As for the prophet, he is a human being to whom a law is revealed, whether or not he is commanded to convey it. So the prophet is absolutely more general than the messenger. This is the relied-upon view. Opposing it are two views: The first is that the messenger is a human being to whom a law is revealed and who has a book. Thus for a messenger, a book and a legal code are necessary. It does not necessarily follow that if he has a book he has a legal code, because it is possible that what is in the book is exhortations. This view was objected to on grounds that books are few and messengers are many, so how can it be required that a messenger have a book? The second view says that for a messenger, one of two things is necessary: either he has a book or his legal code abrogates the legal code of those before him. So if the Torah was revealed to Moses, and revelation came to a prophet of the Children of Israel, for example, to convey its rulings, and no book was revealed to him and his legal code did not abrogate the code of Moses, then he is not a messenger. End of the gloss of the verifying scholar al-Dasuqi on the commentary of Umm al-Barahin of al-Sanusi. It is among the greatest glosses and most beneficial. Imam al-Halimi said in al-Minhaj concerning the meaning of prophethood (nubuwwa) and the difference between the prophet and the messenger: 'Prophethood is a noun derived from al-naba' which is news. However, what is meant in this context is a specific news, which is that by which Allah the Mighty and Majestic obligates one of His servants, distinguishing him by casting it to him from others, and making him know His law, including what is of command, prohibition, exhortation, guidance, promise, and threat. Thus prophethood on this basis is the news and knowledge of the described matters that I have mentioned, and the prophet is the one who conveys them. If, in addition to this success, he is commanded to convey them to people and call them to it, then he is a prophet and a messenger. If it is cast to him to act upon it in his private capacity, and he is not commanded to convey it and call to it, then he is a prophet and not a messenger. So every messenger is a prophet, and not every prophet is a messenger.' End. (2) In reality, we must here further clarify the difference between prophethood and messengership, and explain the view of our scholars who followed this definition, contrary to those who rushed and judged that they are mistaken in this definition, i.e., in the definition of the prophet and messenger, and implied to readers that their statement is not based on any evidence at all, but rather is extreme in blind imitation, pure error, and contradiction of the clear Quran! Even though it is not the way of Ahl al-Sunnah to be decisive about a matter as long as there is some possibility in it! And if this objection had come from opponents among the corporealists (mujassima), it would not require us to go into detail about the issue here, but this statement came from someone who claims affiliation with Ahl al-Sunnah, so it calls for astonishment. We will mention a brief statement here because detail is not appropriate, in which we clarify and explain the aforementioned definition, and for detail there is another place. So we say, and by Allah is success: We will explain below how the scholars understood the gradual stages of our master Muhammad (SAW) in the ranks of prophethood and messengership, so that it may become clear to the generous reader what they intend by the difference between the prophet and the messenger. Whoever is ignorant of this gradual stages finds it easy to oppose their distinction.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion of the definitions of 'prophet' (nabi) and 'messenger' (rasul). It begins by stating the standard Ash'ari position: a messenger is a prophet commanded to convey the revelation, so every messenger is a prophet but not every prophet is a messenger. It then notes two alternative views from a gloss by al-Dasuqi: the first requires a messenger to have a book (objected as rare), the second requires either a book or an abrogating law. Then it quotes Imam al-Halimi's definition, emphasizing that prophethood is news from God, and the distinction is whether the prophet is commanded to convey. The author then defends this definition against critics who claim it contradicts the Quran, arguing that the gradual stages of Muhammad's own prophethood illustrate the difference: Muhammad first became a prophet (naby) and later a messenger (rasul). Thus the definitions are consistent with his example. The passage ends by promising to detail these stages.

Key Terms
نبي
رسول
شريعة
كتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the terms 'prophet' and 'messenger' are interchangeable or that the distinction is not important.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the distinction is essential and based on whether the individual is commanded to convey the revelation. Not all prophets are messengers, but all messengers are prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon definition in this passage, what is the key difference between a prophet and a messenger?
A) A messenger always has a book, while a prophet may not.
B) A prophet is infallible, while a messenger is not.
C) A messenger is commanded to convey the revelation to people, while a prophet is not necessarily commanded to do so.
D) A messenger can abrogate previous laws, while a prophet cannot.

Key Takeaway
The standard Ash'ari definition distinguishes a prophet (nabi) from a messenger (rasul) based on the command to convey the revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the prophets in the Quran, do you consciously distinguish those who were commanded to convey a message from those who were not? How will this understanding change the way you engage with their stories? Commit to studying the life of at least one messenger this week with the intention of carrying his message to others.
?
The relied-upon definition emphasizes that a messenger is defined by the command to convey, not by having a book. Does your life reflect the urgency of conveying the message of Islam? In what specific situation will you take a concrete step to share what you know about Allah's revelation, beginning today?
?
Scholarly disagreements exist, but the passage presents a relied-upon view. Are you building your faith on the strongest foundations, or do you follow weak opinions because they are convenient? Resolve to seek out the relied-upon positions in the matters of creed and base your certainty on them.

Prophethood
beginner
3 min
Imam al-Halimi's Definition of Prophethood and Messengership
Al-Halimi defines prophethood and messengership, emphasizing the command to convey.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Halimi's lexical and theological explanation of the terms 'prophet' and 'messenger' and how he emphasizes the command to convey as the distinguishing factor.

Core Idea
Prophethood is news from God; messengership is delivering that news to others.

Imam al-Halimi, a renowned scholar, provides a detailed definition of prophethood and messengership in his work al-Minhaj. His explanation reinforces the standard Ash'ari distinction by highlighting the linguistic root and the requirement of conveying the message. In this lesson, we examine his precise formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the passage where al-Halimi defines prophethood (nubuwwa) as derived from 'news' (naba') and the specific news that Allah gives to a chosen servant. Notice how he links it to knowledge of the law.
2
Understand the distinction
Al-Halimi then explains that if the prophet is commanded to convey and call people, he becomes a messenger; otherwise he remains a prophet only. This matches the earlier definition.

Prophethood

Defending the Ash'ari Distinction Between Prophet and Messenger
The author defends the standard definition and criticizes those who reject it without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's defense of the standard Ash'ari definition against critics who claim it contradicts the Quran, and his argument that the gradual stages of Prophet Muhammad's mission illustrate the distinction.

Core Idea
The distinction is rooted in the example of Prophet Muhammad's own gradual mission.

Some critics have argued that the Ash'ari definition of prophet and messenger is baseless and contradicts the Quran. In this lesson, we examine the author's rebuttal, which points to the gradual stages of prophethood and messengership in the life of Prophet Muhammad. The author emphasizes that the definition is well-supported and that haste in rejecting it stems from ignorance of this gradual development.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's criticism of the critics
The author begins by expressing astonishment that someone claiming to be from Ahl al-Sunnah would assert that the standard definition has no evidence. He notes that such certainty is not the way of Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Understand the argument from gradual stages
The author promises to explain how scholars understood the gradual stages of Prophet Muhammad's prophetic career, which shows that he was first a prophet (after first revelation) and only later a messenger (when commanded to convey).
This is the core of the defense.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Why does the author say that only those ignorant of this gradual process object? Consider the importance of understanding the Prophet's biography in theological definitions.

البراهين للسنوسي وهي من أعظم الحواشي وأكثرها فوائد وقد قال الإمام الحليمي في المنهاج في معنى النبوة والفرق بين النبي والرسول إن النبوة اسم مشتق من النبأ وهو الخبر إلا أن المراد به في هذا الموضع خبر خاص وهو الذي يلزم الله عز وجل به أحداً من عباده فيميزه بإلقائه إليه عن غيره ويوقفه به على شريعته بما فيها من أمر ونهي ووعظ وإرشاد ووعد ووعيد فتكون النبوة على هذا الخبر والمعرفة بالمخبرات الموصوفة التي ذكرتها والنبي هو المخبر بها فإن انضاف إلى هذا التوفيق أمر تبليغه إلى الناس ودعائهم إليه كان نبياً رسولاً وإن ألقي إليه ليعمل به في خاصته ولم يؤمر بتبليغه والدعاء إليه كان نبياً ولم يكن رسولاً فكل رسول نبي وليس كل نبي رسولاً أهـ (س) (٢) في الحقيقة لا بد هنا من أن نوضح زيادة توضيح الفرق بين النبوة والرسالة ونوجه قول علمائنا الذين مشوا على هذا التعريف خلافاً لمن تسرع وجزم بغلطهم في هذا التعريف أي في تعريف النبي والرسول وأوحى إلى القراء أن قولهم ليس بمستند إلى حجة مطلقاً بل هو في غايته مبالغة في التقليد وخطأ محض ومخالفة لصريح القرآن مع أنه ليس من شأن أهل السنة الجزم بالأمر ما دام فيه بعض الاحتمال ولو أن هذا الاعتراض صدر من قبل المخالفين من المجسمة لما استدعى هذا أن نفصل في المسألة هنا ولكن صدر هذا القول ممن ينتسب إلى أهل السنة فاقتضى العجب وسوف نذكر كلاماً مختصراً هنا لأنه لا يليق التفصيل نبين ونوجه فيه التعريف المذكور أعلاه وللتفصيل محل آخر فنقول وبالله التوفيق سنبين فيما يلي كيف فهم العلماء تدرج سيدنا محمد في مراتب النبوة والرسالة ليتبين للقارئ الكريم ما الذي يريدونه بالفرق بين النبي والرسول ومن جهل هذا التدرج سهل عليه مخالفتهم في تفريقهم
For creation: the messenger (rasul) is more specific than the prophet (nabi), and the prophet is more general. So every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. Some prophets are messengers and some prophets are not messengers if they are not commanded to convey. (1) As for the prophet, he is a human being to whom a law is revealed, whether or not he is commanded to convey it. So the prophet is absolutely more general than the messenger. This is the relied-upon view. Opposing it are two views: The first is that the messenger is a human being to whom a law is revealed and who has a book. Thus for a messenger, a book and a legal code are necessary. It does not necessarily follow that if he has a book he has a legal code, because it is possible that what is in the book is exhortations. This view was objected to on grounds that books are few and messengers are many, so how can it be required that a messenger have a book? The second view says that for a messenger, one of two things is necessary: either he has a book or his legal code abrogates the legal code of those before him. So if the Torah was revealed to Moses, and revelation came to a prophet of the Children of Israel, for example, to convey its rulings, and no book was revealed to him and his legal code did not abrogate the code of Moses, then he is not a messenger. End of the gloss of the verifying scholar al-Dasuqi on the commentary of Umm al-Barahin of al-Sanusi. It is among the greatest glosses and most beneficial. Imam al-Halimi said in al-Minhaj concerning the meaning of prophethood (nubuwwa) and the difference between the prophet and the messenger: 'Prophethood is a noun derived from al-naba' which is news. However, what is meant in this context is a specific news, which is that by which Allah the Mighty and Majestic obligates one of His servants, distinguishing him by casting it to him from others, and making him know His law, including what is of command, prohibition, exhortation, guidance, promise, and threat. Thus prophethood on this basis is the news and knowledge of the described matters that I have mentioned, and the prophet is the one who conveys them. If, in addition to this success, he is commanded to convey them to people and call them to it, then he is a prophet and a messenger. If it is cast to him to act upon it in his private capacity, and he is not commanded to convey it and call to it, then he is a prophet and not a messenger. So every messenger is a prophet, and not every prophet is a messenger.' End. (2) In reality, we must here further clarify the difference between prophethood and messengership, and explain the view of our scholars who followed this definition, contrary to those who rushed and judged that they are mistaken in this definition, i.e., in the definition of the prophet and messenger, and implied to readers that their statement is not based on any evidence at all, but rather is extreme in blind imitation, pure error, and contradiction of the clear Quran! Even though it is not the way of Ahl al-Sunnah to be decisive about a matter as long as there is some possibility in it! And if this objection had come from opponents among the corporealists (mujassima), it would not require us to go into detail about the issue here, but this statement came from someone who claims affiliation with Ahl al-Sunnah, so it calls for astonishment. We will mention a brief statement here because detail is not appropriate, in which we clarify and explain the aforementioned definition, and for detail there is another place. So we say, and by Allah is success: We will explain below how the scholars understood the gradual stages of our master Muhammad (SAW) in the ranks of prophethood and messengership, so that it may become clear to the generous reader what they intend by the difference between the prophet and the messenger. Whoever is ignorant of this gradual stages finds it easy to oppose their distinction.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion of the definitions of 'prophet' (nabi) and 'messenger' (rasul). It begins by stating the standard Ash'ari position: a messenger is a prophet commanded to convey the revelation, so every messenger is a prophet but not every prophet is a messenger. It then notes two alternative views from a gloss by al-Dasuqi: the first requires a messenger to have a book (objected as rare), the second requires either a book or an abrogating law. Then it quotes Imam al-Halimi's definition, emphasizing that prophethood is news from God, and the distinction is whether the prophet is commanded to convey. The author then defends this definition against critics who claim it contradicts the Quran, arguing that the gradual stages of Muhammad's own prophethood illustrate the difference: Muhammad first became a prophet (naby) and later a messenger (rasul). Thus the definitions are consistent with his example. The passage ends by promising to detail these stages.

Key Terms
النبوة
الرسالة
التدرج
المجسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some critics think the Ash'ari distinction between prophet and messenger is arbitrary and without Quranic support.
Correction: The author argues that the distinction is grounded in the gradual stages of Prophet Muhammad's mission and that scholars have robust evidence for it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author find the criticism of the definition particularly objectionable?
A) Because it comes from corporealists (mujassima).
B) Because it comes from someone claiming to be from Ahl al-Sunnah.
C) Because it is based on a hadith.
D) Because it is too detailed.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari distinction is defended by pointing to the gradual stages of Prophet Muhammad's own prophethood and messengership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a scholarly distinction that you don't immediately understand, do you pause to investigate its basis, or do you dismiss it as unnecessary? How does your reaction align with the author's criticism of hasty rejection?
?
The author laments that criticism came from someone claiming to be from Ahl al-Sunnah. Have you ever opposed a well-established teaching out of personal opinion rather than evidence? What might you need to change in your approach to knowledge?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with the knowledge that you rejected a valid definition without proper proof—simply because you preferred your own view. Would that be a defense or a regret? How can you commit to verifying before opposing?

The Gradual Stages of Prophethood: First Four Ranks
Sanusi describes the first four ranks of Muhammad's gradual commissioning as prophet and messenger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four stages through which Allah gradually prepared and informed Muhammad of his prophethood and messengership, as described by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Prophethood was unveiled gradually to confirm sincerity and prepare the Prophet for his mission.

Sanusi presents a detailed account of how Allah prepared Muhammad for prophethood over time. Rather than a sudden event, the commissioning came through distinct stages that built certainty and avoided any accusation of invention. The first four stages—true dreams, purification, indirect glad tidings, and direct angelic visitation—each served a specific purpose in preparing the Prophet's heart and mind for the weight of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in full
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's careful progression from preparation to the four ranks.
The passage is long but forms a cohesive argument.
2
Identify the divine preparation
Find the part that describes why Allah made seclusion beloved and how this prevented affectation. Understand the wisdom behind the delay.
This sets the stage for gradual commissioning.
3
Enumerate the first four stages
The passage lists six stages but details the first four. List them and note what distinguishes each: dreams, purification, indirect glad tidings, direct revelation.
4
Analyze the transition between stages
Notice how each stage increases certainty and prepares the Prophet for the next. The fourth stage gives direct vision but still no command to warn.
This shows the distinction between nabi and rasul.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why did Allah not give the full revelation all at once? The passage gives reasons: to avoid shock, train the Prophet, and provide a model for the community.
This connects to the core idea of gradualness.
