Belief in Known and Unknown Prophets
Obligates detailed belief in known prophets and general belief in others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between detailed (tafsili) and general (ijmali) belief in prophets.

Core Idea
We must know the known prophets and trust the unknown ones.

The obligation to believe in prophets is not limited to those named in revelation. This lesson explains that we must believe in detail in the prophets whose names are known from the Qur'an and Sunnah, and in general in all prophets whom Allah has sent, whether we know their names or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Notice the two categories: detailed belief (tafsilan) and general belief (ijmalan).
The passage lists several prophets by name as examples.
2
Understand 'detailed belief'
We must know and believe in each prophet whose name has reached us through authentic revelation.
These include the 25 prophets mentioned in the Quran and others known from hadith.
3
Understand 'general belief'
For prophets not mentioned, we affirm in general that they are true prophets sent by Allah.
This covers all prophets in Allah's knowledge, even if unknown to us.
4
Reflect on the examples
Notice the prophets listed: Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Ayyub, Ya'qub, Yusuf. These are well-known figures.
The phrase 'and others' indicates there are more known prophets beyond these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب الإيمان تفصيلا بمن اشتهر منهم اسمه بالكتاب والسنة كمحمد وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى وأيوب ويعقوب ويوسف وغيرهم ، وإجمالاً لمن لم يشتهر بأن تعتقد أن كل ما في علم الله من نبي فهو حق ، واختلف هل يُعْلَمُ عدد الأنبياء والرسل أم لا؟ ، فقيل لا يعلم عددهم لقوله تعالى مِنْهُم مَّن قَصَصْنَا عَلَيْكَ وَمِنْهُم مَّن لَّمْ نَقْصُصْ عَلَيْكَ ) [غافر: ۱۷۸، وقيل يعلم عدد الأنبياء والرسل ؟ لما في بعض الروايات من حديث أبي ذر قال قلت يا رسول الله كم عدد الأنبياء والرسل ، قال : "مائة ألف نبي وأربعة عشر ألفا" قلت : يا رسو...
And it is obligatory to believe in detail concerning those whose names are well-known through the Book and the Sunnah, such as Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Ayyub, Ya'qub, Yusuf, and others, and in general concerning those not well-known, by believing that every prophet that is in Allah's knowledge is true. And there is disagreement: are the numbers of prophets and messengers known or not? For it is said that their number is not known because of His statement: 'Of them are those We have narrated to you and of them are those We have not narrated to you' (Qur'an 40:78). And it is said that the number of prophets and messengers is known because of what is in some narrations from the hadith of Abu Dharr: he said, 'I said, O Messenger of Allah, what is the number of prophets and messengers?' He said: 'One hundred thousand prophets and fourteen thousand.' I said, 'O Messenger...'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the obligation to believe in prophets in two modes: detailed (tafsilan) for those whose names are known from revelation (e.g., Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Ayyub, Ya'qub, Yusuf), and general (ijmalan) for those not known, by affirming that every prophet in Allah's knowledge is true. It then introduces a scholarly disagreement on whether the exact number of prophets and messengers can be known. Two positions are given: (1) the number is not known, citing Qur'an 40:78 which states that some prophets are mentioned and some are not; (2) the number is known, citing a hadith from Abu Dharr in which the Prophet reportedly gives numbers: 124,000 prophets and 313 messengers. The text begins to quote the hadith but is cut off at page 121; the continuation is on page 122.

Misconception: Only the 25 prophets named in the Qur'an are authentic prophets.
Correction: The passage requires belief in all prophets, whether named or not, as long as they are in Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Deny them since they are not explicitly mentioned
B) Believe in them in a general way
C) Research their names
D) Only believe in the twenty-five named prophets

Key Takeaway
We must believe in detail in prophets known from revelation, and in general in those unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the unknown prophets (whom you will never know by name) as real individuals with a mission from Allah, or do they remain abstract concepts in your heart? What would change if you truly believed that every prophet in Allah's knowledge is 'true'?

Are the Numbers of Prophets Known?
Presents disagreement on whether the number of prophets/messengers is known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether the exact number of prophets and messengers is known.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree whether the exact number of prophets is known.

After establishing the obligation to believe in all prophets, scholars debated whether the total number of prophets and messengers is knowable. This lesson presents two positions: some say the number is not known, citing a Qur'anic verse; others say it is known, based on a hadith from Abu Dharr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two opinions and their evidence.
The passage presents a disagreement (ikhtilaf).
2
Identify the first opinion
The first opinion holds that the number is not known, supported by Qur'an 40:78: 'Of them are those We have narrated to you and of them are those We have not narrated to you.'
This implies that some prophets are not mentioned, so the total is hidden.
3
Identify the second opinion
The second opinion holds that the number is known, based on a hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) reportedly stated: 'One hundred thousand prophets and fourteen thousand messengers.'
Note: the hadith is from Abu Dharr and its authenticity is debated among scholars.
4
Reflect on the disagreement
Consider why scholars differ: the Qur'an indicates some prophets are not narrated, while the hadith provides specific numbers. Both are used as evidence.
The passage does not settle the debate; it simply presents both views.

The Hadith on the Number of Prophets and Messengers
Quotes and verifies the hadith on the count of prophets and messengers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the content and authenticity of the hadith that specifies the numbers of prophets and messengers.

Core Idea
The hadith gives numbers but its chain is unique, requiring caution.

This lesson examines a key hadith cited in discussions about the number of prophets and messengers. The narration from Abu Dharr is recorded by al-Bayhaqi and al-Hakim. We will look at its text and the scholarly note on its transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the numbers mentioned and the note about the chain.
The text includes a footnote marker (س) indicating editorial comment.
2
Identify the numbers
The hadith gives two numbers: total prophets and messengers among them. Find these in the text.
The numbers are مائة ألف نبي وأربعة وعشرون ألف نبي and ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عشر.
3
Consider the chain note
The text says 'تفرد به يحيى بن سعيد السعيدي' meaning the hadith is unique to this narrator. This affects its authenticity.
Scholars often consider such hadith weak unless corroborated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رواه البيهقي في سننه الكبرى عن أبي ذر قال : دخلت على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو في المسجد فذكر الحديث إلى أن قال : فقلت يا رسول الله كم النبيون؟ قال مائة ألف نبي وأربعة وعشرون ألف نبي قلت : كم المرسلون منهم ؟ قال : ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عشر تفرد به يحيى بن سعيد السعيدي. ****.. ورواه الحاكم في المستدرك .(س).
Al-Bayhaqi narrated it in his Sunan al-Kubra from Abu Dharr: he said: 'I entered upon the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, while he was in the mosque, and he mentioned the hadith until he said: So I said, O Messenger of Allah, how many prophets? He said: one hundred thousand prophets and twenty-four thousand prophets.' I said: 'How many messengers among them?' He said: 'three hundred and thirteen.' Unique to Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Sa'idi. And al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a citation of a hadith from Abu Dharr, recorded by al-Bayhaqi in his Sunan al-Kubra and by al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak. According to the narration, the Prophet stated that there are 124,000 prophets, and among them 313 messengers. The text notes that this hadith is uniquely transmitted by Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Sa'idi, indicating a potential weakness in its chain. The passage serves as evidence for those who hold that the number of prophets is known, as mentioned in the previous lesson. The footnote marker (س) likely indicates a comment from the editor or author about the hadith's authenticity.

Ulul Azm: The Prophets of Firm Resolve
Lists the five ulul azm and additional prophets from al-Kashshaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity of the ulul azm prophets and the scholarly difference regarding their number.

Core Idea
The ulul azm are five core prophets, but some scholars include additional ones.

This passage introduces the concept of ulul azm, the prophets of firm resolve. According to Ibn 'Atiyyah, they are five specific prophets. However, al-Kashshaf expands the list to ten, reflecting a difference of scholarly interpretation. Understanding this discussion helps clarify the Qur'anic term and the status of these prophets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five ulul azm from Ibn 'Atiyyah
Read the first part of the passage to learn the five prophets considered ulul azm by Ibn 'Atiyyah. Notice each name: Muhammad, Noah, Ibrahim, Musa, and Isa.
These are well-known major prophets.
2
Learn the additional five from al-Kashshaf
Read the second part where al-Kashshaf adds five more: Ya'qub, Ishaq, Yusuf, Dawud, and Ayyub. Compare with the first list.
Al-Kashshaf is a famous tafsir by Zamakhshari.
3
Understand the nature of the difference
Reflect on why scholars might differ. The term 'ulul azm' appears in the Qur'an, but its exact referents are interpreted differently. The passage does not resolve the issue but presents both views.
This shows the diversity of scholarly opinion in tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولو العزم منهم خمسة على ما ذكره ابن عطية وهم محمد ونوح وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى صلاة الله عليهم وسلامه عليهم أجمعين، وزاد الكشاف خمسة وهم يعقوب وإسحاق ويوسف وداود وأيوب
The ulul azm among them are five, according to what Ibn 'Atiyyah mentioned: they are Muhammad, Noah, Ibrahim, Musa, and Isa, may Allah's prayers and peace be upon them all. And al-Kashshaf added five: Ya'qub, Ishaq, Yusuf, Dawud, and Ayyub.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines the ulul azm (the prophets of firm resolve) as five: Muhammad, Noah, Ibrahim, Musa, and Isa, based on Ibn 'Atiyyah's commentary. It then mentions that al-Kashshaf (the tafsir of al-Zamakhshari) added five more: Ya'qub, Ishaq, Yusuf, Dawud, and Ayyub. The passage presents a scholarly difference: the core five are agreed upon, but al-Kashshaf expands the list. This reflects differing interpretations of the Qur'anic term 'ulul azm'.

The Sacrifice: Ishmael or Isaac?
Note affirming Ishmael as the intended sacrifice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic position that the son Abraham was commanded to sacrifice was Ishmael, and the scholarly consensus supporting this view.

Core Idea
The sacrifice is Ishmael, not Isaac – affirmed by Qur'an and scholars.

This brief note clarifies a debated point in prophetic history. While Biblical tradition often identifies Isaac as the intended sacrifice, Islamic scripture and scholarly consensus point to Ishmael. The passage cites Imam al-Shafi'i and a poetic couplet to reinforce this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the note carefully
Notice that this is a 'ملاحظة' (note) stating the correct view. The author uses it to settle a doctrinal point.
Pay attention to the poetic couplet; it serves as additional evidence.
2
Identify the sources cited
The note mentions Imam al-Shafi'i and 'many scholars' as holding this view, and then quotes a poem. Identify how each source supports the claim.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? Abraham's test is a key prophetic event. Knowing which son was involved affects our understanding of the story and its moral lessons.
This also relates to the status of Ishmael and Isaac in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : الصحيح أن الذبيح هو إسماعيل وهذا هو ما عليه الشافعي وكثير من العلماء، وقد قال بعض العلماء نظما : إن الذبيح هديت إسماعيل نطق الكتاب بذاك والتنزيل شرف به خص الإله نبينا وأبانته التفسير والتأويل
Note: The correct view is that the intended sacrifice (al-dhabih) is Ishmael, and this is what al-Shafi'i and many scholars hold. And some scholars have composed poetry: 'Indeed the sacrifice, you were guided, is Ishmael; the Book and the Revelation spoke of that. With it Allah honored our Prophet, and exegesis and interpretation made it evident.'
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an editorial note (ملاحظة) stating the correct view regarding the identity of the son whom Abraham was commanded to sacrifice. It asserts that it was Ishmael, not Isaac, and that this is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i and many scholars. A poetic couplet is cited in support, emphasizing that the Qur'an, exegesis, and interpretation confirm Ishmael. The footnote likely attributes the poem or adds commentary. This clarifies a debated issue in prophetic history.

Key Terms
الذبيح
إسماعيل
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the intended sacrifice was Isaac, based on Biblical tradition.
Correction: Islamic consensus, as stated by al-Shafi‘i and many scholars, affirms it was Ishmael, supported by the Qur’an and its interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic view, supported by al-Shafi‘i and many scholars, identifies Ishmael as the son Abraham was commanded to sacrifice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The poem says Allah honored our Prophet through this event. How does knowing that your own lineage (if you are a Muslim) traces back to that sacrificial moment affect the way you see yourself in relation to the story of Abraham? Are you living as if that honor is yours to carry?
?
The source states that the correct view is held by al-Shafi'i and many scholars. Is there an area of your religious practice where you trust scholarly consensus over your own inclination? If not, what is stopping you from submitting to what is 'الصحيح'?

Number of Messengers and the Prophets of Firm Resolve
The author cites a hadith on the number of messengers and lists the prophets of firm resolve.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith establishing the total number of messengers (313) and the differing scholarly opinions on which prophets are designated as Ulu al-Azm (prophets of firm resolve).

Core Idea
The prophets of firm resolve are a select few among the 313 messengers.

This lesson examines a key hadith that provides the total number of messengers sent by Allah. It then presents two scholarly views on which prophets are considered the most resolute (Ulu al-Azm), highlighting a point of interpretive difference within the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith on messenger count
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) is asked about the number of messengers. Note the specific number given: 313.
The number is significant in kalam discussions about the extent of prophethood.
2
Identify Ibn Atiyyah's list of Ulu al-Azm
After the hadith, the author mentions that according to Ibn Atiyyah, the prophets of firm resolve are five: Muhammad, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus. Understand that this is one scholarly opinion.
Ibn Atiyyah is a renowned Quran commentator.
3
Compare with al-Kashshaf's alternative list
The author then notes that al-Kashshaf (al-Zamakhshari) adds five more prophets: Jacob, Isaac, Joseph, David, and Job. Reflect on why there might be a difference of opinion and what criteria could define Ulu al-Azm.
This shows that the classification is not universally agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رسول الله كم الرسل منهم ؟ قال : ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عشر جم غفير ، أولو العزم منهم خمسة على ما ذكره ابن عطية (۳) وهم محمد ونوح وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى صلاة الله عليهم وسلامه عليهم أجمعين، وزاد الكشاف خمسة وهم يعقوب وإسحاق ويوسف وداود وأيوب(4).
The Messenger of Allah: 'How many messengers are among them?' He said: 'Three hundred and thirteen, a great multitude.' The prophets of firm resolve (Ulu al-Azm) among them are five according to what Ibn Atiyyah mentioned (3): they are Muhammad, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus, peace and blessings of Allah be upon them all. And al-Kashshaf added five: Jacob, Isaac, Joseph, David, and Job (4).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a narration of a hadith concerning the number of messengers and the identification of the prophets of firm resolve (Ulu al-Azm). The author begins by quoting a question posed to the Prophet about the number of messengers among the prophets. The Prophet responds that the messengers are three hundred and thirteen, describing them as a great multitude. Then the author adds, based on the interpretation of Ibn Atiyyah, that the prophets of firm resolve among them are five: Muhammad, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus. He then notes that al-Kashshaf (likely the commentary al-Kashshaf by al-Zamakhshari) added five more: Jacob, Isaac, Joseph, David, and Job. This shows a difference of opinion among scholars regarding the exact list of Ulu al-Azm. The passage thus presents the hadith evidence for the number of messengers and a scholarly view on which prophets are considered the most resolute.

Key Terms
رسول (messenger)
أولو العزم (prophets of firm resolve)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all prophets are Ulu al-Azm (prophets of firm resolve).
Correction: The passage shows that only a select few are designated as Ulu al-Azm, with differing lists among scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, how many messengers are there?
A) 124,000
B) 313
C) 5
D) 10

Key Takeaway
The hadith establishes that there are 313 messengers, and the designation of Ulu al-Azm is limited to a few prophets, with scholarly disagreement on the exact list.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
These five Ulu al-Azm prophets faced trials that tested their very souls. Which trial of one of them do you find most challenging to your own faith, and what specific step will you take this week to strengthen your resolve in that area?
?
Allah sent only 313 messengers out of countless prophets. How does this selectivity affect your awe of those chosen? Reflect on your own calling: are you responding to the small responsibilities Allah has given you with the seriousness of a messenger?
?
Scholars disagree on the exact list of Ulu al-Azm. How do you respond when you encounter legitimate differences among scholars? Is it with humility and a desire for truth, or with dismissal and arrogance? Commit to one way you will seek knowledge from differing opinions.

The Sacrificial Son is Ishmael
Affirmation that Ishmael is the sacrificial son with scholarly and scriptural support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that the son Abraham was commanded to sacrifice was Ishmael, not Isaac, and the evidence from scholarship and Qur'anic interpretation.

Core Idea
Ishmael, not Isaac, was the sacrificial son destined by Allah.

The story of Abraham's near-sacrifice of his son is a foundational narrative in Islam, but who was that son? While some traditions identify Isaac, the Sunni scholarly consensus, especially in the Shafi'i school, holds that it was Ishmael. This lesson presents the author's clear affirmation and a poetic reinforcement of that view.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's observation
Start by reading the Arabic observation: 'الصحيح أن الذبيح هو إسماعيل'. Notice the author's decisive language—he says 'the correct view' (al-sahih).
This is a doctrinal claim, not just an opinion.
2
Understand the scholarly backing
The author mentions that this is the position of al-Shafi'i and many scholars. Ask yourself: why does citing a madhab founder matter in Islamic theology?
Al-Shafi'i is a major authority in fiqh and usul.
3
Examine the poetic evidence
Read the verse that follows the observation. It states that the Qur'an and Revelation speak of Ishmael, and that exegesis and interpretation make it clear. Consider how poetry serves as a mnemonic for doctrinal points.
The poem also connects Ishmael to the honor of the Prophet Muhammad, as Ishmael is his ancestor.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter? It affects our understanding of prophetic lineage and the continuity of divine trials. It also illustrates how tafsir (exegesis) and ta'wil (interpretation) are used to resolve apparent ambiguities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : الصحيح أن الذبيح هو إسماعيل وهذا هو ما عليه الشافعي وكثير من العلماء، وقد قال بعض العلماء نظما : إن الذبيح هديت إسماعيل نطق الكتاب بذاك والتنزيل شرف به خَص الإله نبينا وأَبانَهُ التفسير والتأويل
Observation: The correct view is that the sacrificial son (al-dhabih) is Ishmael, and this is the position of al-Shafi'i and many scholars. Some scholars said in verse: 'Indeed the sacrificial son, you are guided, is Ishmael; the Book and the Revelation speak of that. With it Allah honored our Prophet and distinguished him; exegesis and interpretation made it clear.'
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal note clarifying the identity of the son whom Abraham was commanded to sacrifice. The author states that the correct view is that it was Ishmael, not Isaac. This is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i and many other scholars. To reinforce this, the author quotes a poetic composition by some unnamed scholars, which affirms that the Book (Qur'an) and Revelation indicate that the sacrificial son was Ishmael, and that this fact honors the Prophet Muhammad (as Ishmael is his ancestor) and is made clear by exegesis (tafsir) and interpretation (ta'wil). The poem serves as a mnemonic and doctrinal assertion.

Key Terms
الذبيح (the sacrificial son)
إسماعيل (Ishmael)
التفسير والتأويل (exegesis and interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some traditions, including biblical and some Islamic narratives, identify Isaac as the son Abraham was commanded to sacrifice.
Correction: The author asserts that the correct Islamic view, based on the Qur'an and scholarly consensus (especially in the Shafi'i school), is that it was Ishmael. The poem references exegesis and interpretation to clarify the matter.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which son did Abraham sacrifice?
A) Isaac
B) Ishmael
C) Jacob
D) Joseph

Key Takeaway
The correct Sunni position, upheld by al-Shafi'i and many scholars, is that the sacrificial son was Ishmael, as affirmed by the Qur'an and clarified by exegesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you heard stories about the sacrifice of Abraham, did you ever prefer the popular narrative of Isaac over the scholarly consensus of Ishmael? What does that reveal about your willingness to submit to religious authority? Right now, make a firm intention to investigate and accept the truth even when it challenges what you've always assumed.
?
The poem states that this truth 'honored our Prophet and distinguished him.' How does knowing that your Prophet descends from the very son who was saved from sacrifice affect your love for him? Before you sleep tonight, send an extra salawat upon him as a response to this honor.
?
The author used a poem to make this doctrinal point memorable. What core belief about Allah or His prophets do you need to 'write in verse' in your heart so that it sustains you during trials? Take three minutes right now to craft a single line or phrase you can repeat today to anchor that truth.

The Obligation of Prophetic Truthfulness
Derivation of wajib al-sidq from the testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the testimony 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' necessitates the obligation of truthfulness for prophets, and that this truthfulness means their reports correspond to reality.

Core Idea
Truthfulness is a necessary attribute of prophethood, derived from the very title 'Messenger of Allah'.

The shahada 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' is not merely a statement of belief; it carries deep theological implications. The first consequence the author draws is that all prophets must be truthful, because a messenger from God cannot be a liar. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the derivation
Notice how the author uses the phrase 'it is taken from our statement' to indicate a logical inference from the testimony.
2
Define truthfulness
The passage defines sidq as the correspondence of the prophet's news to reality. Reflect on why this definition is important.
3
Connect to divine attribution
The attribution of 'messenger' to God necessitates truthfulness. Ask: why would a messenger from God necessarily be truthful?
The author's argument is concise but foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤخذ من قولنا محمد رسول الله ، وجوب صدق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام أي مطابقة خبرهم للواقع وإضافة الرسول إلى الله تقتضي وجوب صدقه، ويؤخذ أيضاً استحالة الكذب عليهم. وذلك لأن الإيمان بالرسالة الواجبة بشهادة ما لا يحصى من المعجزات توجب شرعاً الإيمان برسالتهم لأنه جاء بتصديقهم ، وإنْ لَمْ يَصدقوا ، لم يكونوا رسلاً أمناء لمولانا العالم بالخفيات (۳). ويؤخذ من قولنا محمد رسول الله استحالة فعل المنهيات كلها سواء نهي تحريم أو كراهة، وذلك محال على الأنبياء كلهم لأنهم أرسلوا ليعلموا الخلق المبعوثين إليهم بأقوالهم وأفعالهم وسكوتهم فإن فعل أحد من الناس فعلاً وعلم به الرسول أو بلغه وسكت عنه وأقره عليه ولم ينكر على الفاعل فنستدل على جوازه بسكوته، فنفعله وإن كان من جنس العبادات فمطلوب إما وجوباً أو ندباً، وإن كان من جنس العادات فمباح، لأنهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام لم يقرروا أحداً على باطل. فيلزم أن لا يكون في
الخفيات هي مشكلات الأمور وغوامضها بالنسبة للمخلوقين وأما بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى فهو عالم بجميع المعلومات تفصيلاً.
And it is taken from our statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' the obligation of the truthfulness of the messengers, peace be upon them, i.e., the correspondence of their news to reality, and the attribution of the messenger to Allah necessitates the obligation of his truthfulness. And it is also taken the impossibility of lying upon them. That is because belief in the obligatory messengership, by the testimony of countless miracles, obligates legally belief in their messengership, because he (the Prophet) came confirming them, and if they were not truthful, they would not be trustworthy messengers for our Master, the Knower of hidden things (3). And it is taken from our statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' the impossibility of committing all prohibitions, whether prohibition of forbiddance or dislike. And that is impossible for all prophets, because they were sent to teach the creation to whom they were sent by their words, deeds, and silences. For if a person performs an action and the prophet knows it or it reaches him, and he remains silent about it, and acquiesces to it, and does not disapprove of the doer, then we infer its permissibility from his silence, so we do it. And if it is of the type of acts of worship, it is required either as obligation or recommendation, and if it is of the type of customs, it is permissible. Because they, peace be upon them, did not approve anyone upon falsehood. So it follows that there must not be in [incomplete]. Al-Khafiyyat are the problematic and obscure matters for created beings, but as for Allah Almighty, He is Knower of all information in detail.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage extracts doctrinal consequences from the testimony. It states that from 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' we derive the obligation of sidq (truthfulness) for prophets, the impossibility of kadhib (lying), and the impossibility of fi'l al-munhiyat (committing prohibited acts). The argument is that if the prophets were not truthful, they would not be trustworthy messengers. Also, prophets are sent to teach by word and deed; if they remained silent about an action, that indicates permissibility. The footnote explains that 'khafiyyat' refers to hidden matters, which Allah knows fully.

Key Terms
صدق
رسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'sidq' in this passage?
A) Prophets never lie in preaching
B) Their reports correspond to reality
C) They are honest in personal dealings
D) Their teachings are correct

Key Takeaway
The testimony 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' logically implies that all prophets are truthful in all their reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' multiple times a day. Does this statement actually obligate you to accept every single word he spoke as corresponding to reality? If so, which hadith or teaching do you still find yourself resisting, and what would it cost you to submit fully?
?
The passage teaches that the very title 'Messenger of Allah' logically entails truthfulness. Does your claim to be a 'believer' ('abdullah) entail the same kind of truthfulness in your speech? Where is there a gap between what you say and what is real?
?
If the prophet (peace be upon him) were to walk into your life right now and give you one command from the Sunnah that you have been neglecting, would you obey immediately without hesitation? If not, what does that say about your belief in his truthfulness?

The Impossibility of Lying for Prophets
Derivation of istihalat al-kadhib from the testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lying is impossible for prophets based on the testimony and the role of miracles.

Core Idea
Prophets cannot lie because their truthfulness is confirmed by miracles and trustworthiness.

The second derivation from 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' is the impossibility of lying for prophets. This lesson explores the reasoning: if prophets could lie, the countless miracles confirming their message would be deceptive, and they would not be trustworthy. The footnote about 'al-khafiyyat' further emphasizes God's perfect knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second derivation
The author states that the impossibility of lying is also taken from the testimony. Note the word 'also' connecting it to the previous point.
2
Examine the argument

3
Consider the footnote
The footnote explains 'al-khafiyyat' (hidden matters) which God knows in detail. This reinforces God's perfect knowledge, ensuring He would not send a lying messenger.
The footnote is part of the original text.

The Impossibility of Committing Prohibited Acts
Derivation of istihalat al-munhiyat and its basis in prophetic teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts (both haram and makruh) and how their silence indicates permissibility.

Core Idea
Prophets are immune from all sins, because they teach through their actions and silence.

The third derivation from the testimony is that prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts. The author explains that prophets are sent to teach humanity not only by words but also by deeds and even by their silence. If they performed a prohibited act, it would mislead people. Additionally, their silence on an action indicates its permissibility. This lesson explores the reasoning behind prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third derivation
The author states that from the testimony we derive the impossibility of prophets committing any prohibited acts. Notice the inclusiveness 'all prohibitions'.
2
Analyze the teaching role of prophets
Prophets teach by words, deeds, and silence. Think about how each of these modes conveys a message to the community.
3
Explore the implication of silence
The passage explains that a prophet's silence on an action indicates its permissibility. This is a crucial principle in Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since prophets never approve falsehood, they must be free from all prohibited acts. This establishes their 'isma (infallibility).

For if a person performs an action and the prophet knows it or it reaches him, and he remains silent about it, and acquiesces to it, and does not disapprove of the doer, then we infer its permissibility from his silence, so we do it. And if it is of the type of acts of worship, it is required either as obligation or recommendation, and if it is of the type of customs, it is permissible. Because they, peace be upon them, did not approve anyone upon falsehood. So it follows that there must not be in [incomplete]. Al-Khafiyyat are the problematic and obscure matters for created beings, but as for Allah Almighty, He is Knower of all information in detail.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage extracts doctrinal consequences from the testimony. It states that from 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' we derive the obligation of sidq (truthfulness) for prophets, the impossibility of kadhib (lying), and the impossibility of fi'l al-munhiyat (committing prohibited acts). The argument is that if the prophets were not truthful, they would not be trustworthy messengers. Also, prophets are sent to teach by word and deed; if they remained silent about an action, that indicates permissibility. The footnote explains that 'khafiyyat' refers to hidden matters, which Allah knows fully.

Key Terms
منهيات
أنبياء
سكوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe prophets can commit minor sins (saghair) but not major sins.
Correction: This passage states that it is impossible for prophets to commit any prohibited act, whether forbidden (haram) or disliked (makruh), showing their complete infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a prophet's silence on an action indicate?
A) The action is forbidden
B) The action is permissible
C) The action is recommended
D) The action is disliked

Key Takeaway
Prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts because they are sent to teach by their actions and silence, and they never approve falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a situation where you are unsure about an action's ruling, do you instinctively consider the Prophet's silence as a guide? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed his silence is always a sign of permission?
?
If prophets are models in every word, deed, and silence, then reflect on one area of your life where you have ignored the prophetic example. What is that area, and what is one concrete step you will take this week to align your actions with his teaching?
?
The passage says prophets 'did not approve anyone upon falsehood.' Think of a falsehood you have silently condoned—whether in your own heart, in your family, or in your community. Will you remain silent, or will you speak the truth as the prophets did?

Deriving Truthfulness and Impossibility of Lying for Prophets
The commentator derives the obligation of truthfulness and impossibility of lying from the creedal phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' logically implies both the obligation of truthfulness and the impossibility of lying for all prophets.

Core Idea
To be a messenger of Allah, a prophet must be truthful and cannot lie.

The phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' is not just a statement of faith; it carries deep theological implications. From this simple declaration, scholars derive essential attributes of prophets—that they must be truthful and that lying is impossible for them. In this lesson, we examine how the commentator extracts these conclusions from the Shahada itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the original Arabic text and the literal English translation. Try to follow the logic of the argument without yet diving into commentary.
Notice the two key terms: وجوب (obligation) and استحالة (impossibility).
2
Identify the two derived attributes
The commentator derives two necessary attributes of prophets: wajib al-sidq (obligatory truthfulness) and istihalat al-kadhib (impossibility of lying). Locate where each is mentioned in the passage.
The first is explicitly stated in the phrase 'وجوب صدق الرسل', and the second in 'استحالة الكذب عليهم'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Analyze why the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' leads to these conclusions. Note the role of the attribution of 'messenger' to Allah, and the argument from miracles confirming the message.
Key point: if the messenger could lie, he would not be amin (trustworthy), contradicting the purpose of prophethood.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why these attributes are necessary for the credibility of prophethood and why they are derived from the basic testimony of faith.
This shows that even the shortest article of faith contains deep implications about the nature of prophethood.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write the key takeaway in your own words, ensuring you can explain why truthfulness is wajib and lying is muhal for prophets.
Test your understanding with the MCQ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤخذ من قولنا محمد رسول الله ، وجوب صدق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام أي مطابقة خبرهم للواقع وإضافة الرسول إلى الله تقتضي وجوب صدقه، ويؤخذ أيضاً استحالة الكذب عليهم. وذلك لأن الإيمان بالرسالة الواجبة بشهادة ما لا يحصى من المعجزات توجب شرعاً الإيمان برسالتهم لأنه جاء بتصديقهم ، وإنْ لَمْ يَصدقوا ، لم يكونوا رسلاً أمناء لمولانا العالم بالخفيات (۳).
And it is taken from our saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah': the obligation of truthfulness of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, i.e., the correspondence of their news to reality. And the attribution of the messenger to Allah necessitates the obligation of his truthfulness. And it is also taken the impossibility of lying upon them. That is because faith in the obligatory messengership by the testimony of countless miracles obligates legally faith in their message because it came to confirm them, and if they were not truthful, they would not be trustworthy messengers to our Lord, the Knower of subtle things (۳).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a derivation (istinbat) of two necessary attributes of prophets—truthfulness and the impossibility of lying—from the very phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. The commentator explains that the phrase implies wajib al-sidq (obligation of truthfulness) because the term 'Rasul' means one who delivers a message from Allah, and since Allah is all-knowing and truthful, His messenger must be truthful. Furthermore, the same phrase necessitates istihalat al-kadhib (impossibility of lying) for prophets. The reasoning is that faith in the messenger's message is obligatory, supported by countless miracles that confirm him. If he were to lie, he would not be an amin (trustworthy) messenger, contradicting the very purpose of prophethood.

Key Terms
صدق
كذب
رسول
أمانة
معجزات
وجوب
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that prophets could lie for a greater good or to preserve a higher objective.
Correction: The passage establishes that lying is absolutely impossible (muhal) for prophets, as it would contradict the trustworthiness (amanah) required for their role.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' necessarily imply about prophets?
A) They may sometimes lie for a greater good
B) They are truthful and lying is impossible
C) They are infallible only in delivering revelation
D) They are truthful but may err in daily life

Key Takeaway
From the declaration 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah,' we learn that all prophets must be truthful and cannot lie.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, 'Muhammad Rasul Allah,' do you truly internalize that every word he spoke is absolute truth? How does that certainty reshape your response to his commands, especially the ones you find difficult?
?
If prophets cannot lie, then the Prophet's warnings about the grave, judgment, and hell are not mere possibilities but certainties. Are you living today as if those warnings are as real as the ground beneath your feet? What would you change if you truly believed that?
?
The passage says a lying messenger would not be trustworthy. You claim to follow this messenger—do you trust him completely? Is there any teaching of his that you secretly doubt or rationalize away? Confront that doubt now and decide: will you submit fully or keep a reservation?

Impossibility of Prohibited Acts for Prophets
Proving that prophets cannot commit prohibited acts due to their role as teachers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts, whether unlawfulness or offensiveness, and how this is derived from their role as teachers.

Core Idea
Prophets cannot sin because their silence implies approval of actions.

This lesson explores a crucial consequence of believing that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah: the impossibility of prophets committing any prohibited acts. The commentator explains that prophets are sent as teachers through words, actions, and even their silence. Any violation by a prophet would contradict his mission to guide people to what is right.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the argument unfolds from the declaration of prophethood.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the words: منهيات, تحريم, كراهة, سكوت, اقرار. Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage argues that prophets teach through their silence. If a prophet remained silent about a prohibited act, it would be taken as approval. Therefore, prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts themselves.
This includes both haram and makruh acts.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why even makruh acts are impossible. The passage stresses that prophets are chosen for their truthfulness and trustworthiness.
This distinguishes them from ordinary people.
5
Connect to the broader creed
This lesson is part of the attributes derived from the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. See how this fits into the overall belief in prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤخذ من قولنا محمد رسول الله استحالة فعل المنهيات كلها سواء نهي تحريم أو كراهة، وذلك محال على الأنبياء كلهم لأنهم أرسلوا ليعلموا الخلق المبعوثين إليهم بأقوالهم وأفعالهم وسكوتهم فإن فعل أحد من الناس فعلاً وعلم به الرسول أو بلغه وسكت عنه وأقره عليه ولم ينكر على الفاعل فنستدل على جوازه بسكوته، فنفعله وإن كان من جنس العبادات فمطلوب إما وجوباً أو ندباً، وإن كان من جنس العادات فمباح، لأنهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام لم يقروا أحداً على باطل. فيلزم أن لا يكون في جميعها مخالفة لأمر مولانا جل وعز الذي اختارهم على جميع خلقه، واختياره لهم يستلزم وصفهم بالصدق والأمانة وهو تعالى أمنهم على خبر وحيه.
And it is taken from our saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah': the impossibility of performing all prohibitions, whether a prohibition of unlawfulness or offensiveness. And that is impossible for all prophets because they were sent to teach the creatures to whom they were sent through their words, actions, and tacit approvals. For if a person performs an action and the prophet knows about it or it reaches him and he remains silent about it and approves of it and does not denounce the doer, we deduce its permissibility from his silence, so we do it. If it is of the category of acts of worship, it is required either obligatorily or recommendably; if it is of the category of customs, it is permissible. Because they, peace and blessings be upon them, did not approve anyone upon falsehood. Therefore, it is necessary that there be in all of them any violation of the command of our Lord, Mighty and Majestic, who chose them over all His creation. And His choosing of them entails describing them with truthfulness and trustworthiness, and He, exalted be He, entrusted them with the news of His revelation.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage derives another necessary attribute from the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah': istihalat fi'l al-munhiyat (impossibility of committing prohibited acts) for prophets, whether the prohibition is tahrim (unlawful) or karaha (offensive). The commentator argues that prophets are sent to teach their communities by example—through their words, actions, and tacit approvals (sukut). If a prophet were to commit a prohibited act or silently allow a prohibited act, people would infer that it is permissible, leading to contradiction with the prophet's mission. Specifically, if a person does something and the prophet knows and remains silent without denouncing, that silence indicates permissibility. Therefore, if a prophet himself commits a prohibition, he would be contradicting his own teaching. Since Allah has chosen prophets for their truthfulness and trustworthiness (sidq and amana), it is impossible for them to violate His commands.

Key Terms

تحريم
كراهة
سكوت
اقرار
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only major sins are impossible for prophets.
Correction: This passage states that all prohibited acts, including makruh (offensive), are impossible for prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for prophets to commit even offensive (makruh) acts?
A) Because they are superior to all angels
B) Because their silence on actions indicates permissibility, so they cannot silently allow wrong
C) Because Allah has chosen them only for major acts
D) Because they are not subject to any prohibitions

Key Takeaway
Prophets cannot commit any prohibited acts because their silence would imply approval, contradicting their mission to teach truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the prophet's silence on a wrong action would imply approval, what does your own silence on wrongdoing around you say about your faith? Are you tolerating sins in your community that you know the prophet would have denounced?
?
The passage says prophets were chosen for their truthfulness and trustworthiness. As someone who claims to follow them, how trustworthy are you in conveying the deen? Are you careful to speak only the truth about Allah and His messenger, or do you sometimes propagate weak or false teachings?
?
If you found yourself committing a sin, would you be quick to seek forgiveness and rectify it, knowing that persistence in sin contradicts the very example of the prophets? What action will you take today to align your life more closely with their impeccable character?

Preference of Prophets over Angels: The Majority View
The commentator states the majority view on prophets vs angels and the exception of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that prophets are superior to angels, the Mu'tazila objection, and the unique consensus on Prophet Muhammad's absolute superiority.

Core Idea
Prophets are superior to angels, but only Prophet Muhammad is undisputedly the best of creation.

This passage addresses a key theological disagreement: are prophets superior to angels? The commentator presents the majority view, notes the Mu'tazila position, and highlights the consensus that Prophet Muhammad is without exception the best of all creation. Understanding this clarifies an important point of Sunni doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'ونقل الأئمة عليه الإجماع') carefully. Let the Arabic sink in before reading the translation.
Notice the phrase 'وهو قول الجمهور' – it signals the mainstream position.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage states that the majority holds prophets are superior, while the Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna hold angels are superior. Mark these positions.
The disagreement is limited to prophets other than Muhammad.
3
Recognize the exception for Muhammad
The commentator emphasizes that there is no disagreement about Prophet Muhammad: he is the best of all creation, by consensus.
The word 'أما هو' signals the shift to the unique status of Muhammad.
4
Understand the consensus claim
The passage says the imams transmitted ijma' on Muhammad's superiority. This means this point is agreed upon by all scholars.
Consensus is a powerful proof in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : يؤخذ مما ذكرنا من اختيار الله للأنبياء على جميع الخلق تفضيلهم على الملائكة وهو قول الجمهور، وذهب المعتزلة وبعض أهل السنة إلى تفضيل الملائكة على الأنبياء، وهذا الخلاف واقع في غير محمد ، وأما هو وا له له ، فلا خلاف في أنه أفضل من الخلق على الإطلاق، ونقل الأئمة عليه الإجماع. جميع ملاحظة : ذهب الزمخشري إلى تفضيل جبريل على نبينا محمد ، ولذا قال بعض المغاربة : جهل الزمخشري مذهبه ، لأن المعتزلة يقولون أنه أفضل من جميع الأنبياء ومن جميع الملائكة.
Note: It is taken from what we mentioned of Allah's choice of prophets over all creation that they are preferred over angels, and that is the opinion of the majority. The Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna held the preference of angels over prophets. This disagreement concerns other than Muhammad. As for him, there is no disagreement that he is the best of creation absolutely, and the imams have transmitted consensus upon it. All note: al-Zamakhshari held the preference of Jibril over our Prophet Muhammad, and therefore some Maghribis said: al-Zamakhshari is ignorant of his own school, because the Mu'tazila say that he [Muhammad] is better than all prophets and all angels.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a note (mulahaza) on the comparative status of prophets and angels. The commentator says that from the fact that Allah chose prophets over all creation (mentioned earlier), it follows that prophets are superior to angels (tafdiluhum 'ala al-mala'ika). This is the view of the majority (jumhur). However, the Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna held that angels are superior to prophets. The commentator notes that this dispute applies only to prophets other than Muhammad, peace be upon him. For him, there is unanimous consensus (ijma') that he is the best of all creation without exception. The commentator then adds a second note about al-Zamakhshari, who reportedly preferred Jibril over Prophet Muhammad. Some Maghribi scholars commented that al-Zamakhshari is ignorant of his own Mu'tazili school, because the Mu'tazila actually hold that Muhammad is superior to all prophets and all angels.

Key Terms
تفضيل
ملائكة
أنبياء
إجماع
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Muslims agree that prophets are superior to angels without exception.
Correction: While the majority holds this view, the Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna hold angels are superior, but all agree Muhammad is the best.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that angels are superior to prophets?
A) The majority of Ahl al-Sunna
B) The Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna
C) All scholars
D) The Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The majority view is that prophets are superior to angels, but only Prophet Muhammad is universally acknowledged as the best of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly honour Prophet Muhammad as the best of creation in your daily actions, or do you take his status for granted? What is one concrete way you can show you believe he is the best of all creation?
?
The Mu'tazila denied the superiority of prophets except Muhammad. Is there any area of your faith where you accept only part of what Allah has chosen, while rejecting another part? Are you fully submitted to Allah's choices, even when they challenge your intellect?
?
This passage shows that the majority of scholars hold prophets superior to angels. Does your heart feel that reverence? Do you see the prophets as truly worthy of that status, or do you need to strengthen your love for them?

Zamakhshari's Contradiction to His Own School
The commentator refutes Zamakhshari's preference of Jibril over Muhammad as contradictory to Mu'tazili doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Zamakhshari's preference of Jibril over Muhammad contradicts his own Mu'tazili school.

Core Idea
Zamakhshari's view on Jibril over Muhammad shows ignorance of his own school's position.

The commentator adds a second note exposing an inconsistency in al-Zamakhshari's theology. Zamakhshari, a leading Mu'tazili scholar, reportedly held that Jibril is superior to Prophet Muhammad. Yet the Mu'tazila themselves hold that Muhammad is superior to all prophets and angels. This reveals Zamakhshari's deviation from his own school.

Guided Path

2
Identify Zamakhshari's claim
Zamakhshari preferred Jibril over Prophet Muhammad. Note that this is a bold claim.
3
See the Mu'tazili position
The Maghribi scholars pointed out that the Mu'tazila actually say Muhammad is better than all prophets and all angels. So Zamakhshari contradicted his own madhab.
This shows even great scholars can be inconsistent.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this illustrates the importance of adhering to the agreed-upon positions of a school, and that individual scholars can err.

Human Attributes of Prophets: Permissibility and Benefit
Derivation of permissibility of human attributes for prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is permissible for prophets to have human attributes and how these attributes do not diminish their prophethood but rather increase their reward through patience.

Core Idea
Human attributes affirm the prophet's humanity and magnify their patience.

This passage derives a subtle but important point from the testimony 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. By emphasizing messengership rather than divinity, the creed affirms that prophets experience human conditions and that these conditions enhance rather than reduce their rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author moves from the testimony to a logical inference about human attributes.
The key phrase is 'because we have attributed the message and not divinity'.
2
Identify the logical inference
Understand that if we had attributed divinity to the Prophet, human attributes would be impossible. Since we only attribute messengership, human attributes are permissible.
This shows the precision of the testimony in shaping 'aqidah.
3
Reflect on the benefit
Consider how human attributes do not lower the prophet's rank but rather increase it through their patience. This teaches us to respect the prophets in their full humanity.
Patience on hardships is a virtue that elevates their station.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤخذ من قولنا محمد رسول الله جواز الأعراض البشرية عليهم صلاة الله وسلامه عليهم، لأننا أضفنا لنبينا محمد الرسالة وليس الألوهية حتى يلزم استحالة الأعراض البشرية في حقه والعَرَضُ البشري لا يُنقِصُ في رسالتهم ونبوتهم وعُلُوِّ منزلتهم عند الله تعالى بل ذاك مما يزيد فيها بصبرهم عليها .
And it is taken from our saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah': the permissibility of human attributes (al-a'rad al-bashariyya) upon them, peace and blessings of Allah be upon them, because we have attributed to our Prophet Muhammad the message and not divinity, such that the impossibility of human attributes in his regard would be necessitated. And the human attribute does not diminish their message, prophethood, and high station with Allah, exalted be He; rather, that is something that increases it through their patience upon them.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage derives another point from the testimony: the permissibility of human attributes (a'rad bashariyya) for prophets. The commentator explains that in saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', we are attributing risala (messengership), not uluhiyya (divinity). If we had attributed divinity, then it would be impossible for human attributes to apply. Since we only attribute messengership, it is permissible for prophets to have human characteristics such as illness, hunger, etc. Importantly, these human attributes do not detract from their prophethood or high rank; rather, enduring them patiently only increases their reward and status.

Correction: Prophets are human beings and experience human conditions; these do not diminish their prophethood but rather increase their reward due to their patience.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

?
If you believe that your own human weaknesses reduce your rank with Allah, what does this lesson teach you about the real source of elevation? Will you commit to reframing your view of hardships as opportunities for patience and reward?

Superiority of Prophets and Comprehensiveness of the Shahada
The author explains prophetic superiority and the shahada's comprehensiveness with notes on scholarly disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophets are superior to angels, how the shahada encompasses all necessary beliefs, and the implication of calling Muhammad 'Messenger of God' regarding human attributes.

Core Idea
Prophethood and the shahada together teach the core of Islamic creed in the most concise form.

This passage from the Sanusi commentary explains two interconnected points: why prophets (especially Muhammad) are superior to all creation, and how the simple phrase of the shahada contains the entire creed. It also addresses a common objection about human attributes in prophets. By studying this, you will see the depth packed into the foundational declaration of faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of prophetic superiority
The author argues that God's choice of prophets implies their truthfulness and trustworthiness. Note the disagreement with Mu'tazila on whether prophets or angels are superior.
The majority view (Jumhur) holds that prophets are superior, except for Muhammad (PBUH) who is unanimously the best.
2
Recognize the consensus on Muhammad's superiority
The passage clarifies that while there is debate about other prophets, Muhammad (PBUH) and his family are unanimously the best of all creation. The imams have transmitted consensus on this.
This includes the refutation of al-Zamakhshari's view that Gabriel is superior.
3
Derive the permissibility of human attributes
From the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God', we infer that prophets can have human attributes without diminishing their status, because we attribute messengership, not divinity.
Human attributes (aʿrāḍ bashariyya) actually increase their merit through patience.
4
Appreciate the comprehensiveness of the shahada
The shahada, though brief, contains all necessary beliefs about God and His messengers. The Law made it a declaration of the faith in the heart.

All of them are contrary to the command of our Master, the Mighty and Majestic, who chose them over all His creation, and His choosing them necessitates describing them with truthfulness and trustworthiness, and He entrusted them with the news of His revelation. Note: It is taken from what we have mentioned of God's choice of prophets over all creation that they are superior to angels, and this is the view of the majority (Jumhur). The Mu'tazila and some of Ahl al-Sunna inclined to the superiority of angels over prophets, and this disagreement is concerning other than Muhammad (PBUH). As for him and his family, there is no disagreement that he is the best of all creation absolutely, and the imams have transmitted consensus upon it. Perhaps due to its conciseness along with its inclusive nature of what we have mentioned, the Law made it a translation of what is in the heart of faith.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a composite of arguments and notes concerning prophethood and the shahada. It begins by stating that all prophets are chosen by God, which necessitates their truthfulness and trustworthiness. A first note then discusses the superiority of prophets over angels: the majority view (Jumhur) holds that prophets are superior, while the Mu'tazila and some Ahl al-Sunna argue for the superiority of angels. However, this disagreement applies only to prophets other than Muhammad (PBUH) and his family, for whom there is consensus that he is the best of all creation. A second note addresses al-Zamakhshari's view that Gabriel is superior to Muhammad (PBUH), and criticizes him for contradicting his own Mu'tazili school, which holds that prophets are superior to angels. The passage then derives from the phrase 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' that prophets are subject to human attributes (aʿrāḍ bashariyya), because we attribute messengership (risāla) to him, not divinity. These human attributes do not diminish their prophethood but rather increase their merit through patience. Finally, the passage concludes that the shahada, despite its few words, contains all the necessary beliefs about God and His messengers, and the Law made it a declaration of the faith in the heart.

Prophetic Attributes: Necessity, Impossibility, and Permissibility
Sanusi defines the necessary, impossible, and possible traits for prophets with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of prophetic attributes (necessary, impossible, permissible) and their rational and revelatory proofs.

Core Idea
Prophets are defined by what they must be, cannot be, and may be—each with a proof.

After establishing the attributes of Allah, Sanusi turns to the prophets. Just as we know what is necessary, impossible, and possible for Allah, we now learn the same for His messengers. This passage lays out the three categories with their proofs, culminating in the shahada as the summary of all creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the first part where Sanusi lists what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for prophets. Notice the three categories mirror the structure used for Allah's attributes.
2
Understand the proofs
The passage gives separate proofs for truthfulness and trustworthiness (and by extension conveying). Read each proof carefully and see how they rely on logic and revelation.
The proof for trustworthiness also proves conveying.
3
Consider permissible human accidents
Sanusi lists examples like sickness and explains why they occur. Ask yourself: does this diminish the prophet's rank? The answer is no.
4
Reflect on the shahada
The passage ends by tying all these beliefs to the declaration of faith. Think about how the shahada encapsulates the entire creed.

As for the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, what is necessary in their regard is truthfulness, trustworthiness, and conveying what they were commanded to convey to creation. And impossible in their regard, peace and blessings be upon them, are the opposites of these attributes, which are lying, treachery by doing something they were forbidden from with a prohibition of prohibition or dislike, or concealing something they were commanded to convey to creation. And permissible for them, peace and blessings be upon them, is what is from human accidents that do not lead to deficiency in their high ranks, like sickness and the like. As for the proof of the necessity of their truthfulness, peace and blessings be upon them, because if they were not truthful, then lying would necessarily follow in the report of Him, exalted, due to His, exalted, confirming them through the miracle that is in the position of His saying: 'My servant has spoken truthfully in all he conveys from Me.' As for the proof of the necessity of trustworthiness for them, peace and blessings be upon them, because if they were treacherous by doing something forbidden or disliked, then the forbidden or disliked would turn into obedience in their regard, because Allah, exalted, has commanded us to follow them in their words and deeds, and Allah, exalted, does not command the doing of something forbidden or disliked. And this very same is the proof of the necessity of the third. As for the evidence for the permissibility of human accidents upon them, it is the observation of their occurrence from them, either to magnify their rewards, or for legislation, or as consolation from the world, or to indicate the lowliness of its worth with Allah, exalted, and His lack of satisfaction with it as an abode of recompense for His prophets and saints, considering their states therein, peace and blessings be upon them. And the meanings of all these creedal beliefs are encompassed by the statement: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage classifies the attributes of prophets into necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and permissible (jā’iz). The necessary attributes are truthfulness (ṣidq), trustworthiness (amāna), and conveying (tablīgh) what they are commanded. The impossible are their opposites: lying, treachery (doing forbidden or disliked acts, or concealing divine commands). The permissible are human accidents (aʿrāḍ bashariyya) like sickness that do not lower their exalted rank. The proof for truthfulness: if prophets lied, God's confirmation through miracles would imply false information from God. The proof for trustworthiness: if they committed forbidden/disliked acts, then those acts would become obedience (since we are commanded to follow them), but God does not command such acts. This same proof applies to conveying (the third necessary attribute). The proof for permissible human accidents: we observe these from prophets, and they occur for various wise purposes: to increase rewards, provide legal rulings, comfort from worldly attachment, or to highlight the lowliness of this world. Finally, all these creedal beliefs are summarized in the declaration of faith: 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammad Rasul Allah.'

الأمانة
التبليغ
المعجزة
الأعراض البشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human accidents like sickness imply prophets are sinful or deficient.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that such accidents do not lead to deficiency in their high ranks and occur for wise purposes.

Key Takeaway

Prophetic Attributes: Necessary, Impossible, Permissible
Sanusi defines the prophetic attributes: wajib (truthfulness, trustworthiness, delivery), mustahil (their opposites), ja'iz (human accidents).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories of prophetic attributes (necessary, impossible, permissible) and the logical proofs that establish each category.

Core Idea
Prophets must be truthful, trustworthy, and deliver the message; their opposites are impossible; human accidents are permissible.

Sanusi now turns from the attributes of Allah to those of His messengers. He classifies prophetic attributes into three rational categories—necessary, impossible, and permissible—each supported by a clear proof. This passage establishes the theological foundation for prophethood, showing why we can trust the prophets and how their human experiences do not diminish their lofty rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
The passage opens by listing the necessary attributes, then their opposites (impossible), then the permissible ones. Read carefully to see how Sanusi structures each category.
The necessary attributes are truthfulness, trustworthiness, and delivery.
2
Study the proof for truthfulness
Sanusi argues that if prophets were not truthful, God's confirmation via miracles would make God a liar—impossible. Focus on the logic: miracles are like God saying 'My servant is truthful.'
This proof relies on the principle that God cannot lie.
3
Study the proof for trustworthiness and delivery
The proof for trustworthiness applies equally to delivery. If prophets betrayed by committing forbidden acts, those acts would become obligatory by imitating them—impossible. Note how the same reasoning covers the third attribute.
God commands us to follow the prophets, so their actions must be sinless.
4
Understand the permissibility of human accidents
The passage gives observed examples—illness etc.—and explains that these serve divine purposes like reward or teaching. Recognize that these do not imply deficiency.
Human accidents are permissible because they don't affect their moral perfection.

وَأَمَّا الرُّسُلُ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ، فَيَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِمُ الصِّدْقُ وَالْأَمَانَةُ وَتَبْلِيغُ مَا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ، وَيَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ أَضْدَادُ هَذِهِ الصِّفَاتِ، وَهِيَ الْكَذِبُ وَالْخِيَانَةُ بِفِعْلِ شَيْءٍ مِمَّا نُهُوا عَنْهُ نَهْيَ تَحْرِيمٍ أَوْ كَرَاهَةٍ، أَوْ كِتْمَانُ شَيْءٍ مِمَّا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ، وَيَجُوزُ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةَ وَالسَّلَامُ مَا هُوَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْبَشَرِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا تُؤَدِّي إِلَى نَقْصٍ فِي مَرَاتِبِهِمِ الْعَلِيَّةِ كَالْمَرَضِ وَنَحْوِهِ. أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ صِدْقِهِمْ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ، فَلِأَنَّهُمْ لَوْ لَمْ يَصْدُقُوا لَزِمَ الْكَذِبُ فِي خَبَرِهِ تَعَالَى لِتَصْدِيقِهِ تَعَالَى لَهُمْ بِالْمُعْجِزَةِ النَّازِلَةِ مَنْزِلَةَ قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: صَدَقَ عَبْدِي فِي كُلِّ مَا يُبَلِّغُ عَنِّي. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْأَمَانَةِ لَهُمْ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ فَلِأَنَّهُمْ لَوْ خَانُوا بِفِعْلِ مُحَرَّمٍ، أَوْ مَكْرُوهٍ، لَانْقَلَبَ الْمُحَرَّمُ أَوِ الْمَكْرُوهُ طَاعَةً فِي حَقِّهِمْ، لِأَنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى أَمَرَنَا بِالِاقْتِدَاءِ بِهِمْ فِي أَقْوَالِهِمْ وَأَفْعَالِهِمْ، وَلَا يَأْمُرُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى بِفِعْلِ مُحَرَّمٍ وَلَا مَكْرُوهٍ، وَهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ هُوَ بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الثَّالِثِ. وَأَمَّا دَلِيلُ جَوَازِ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْبَشَرِيَّةِ عَلَيْهِمْ فَمُشَاهَدَةُ وُقُوعِهَا مِنْهُمْ إِمَّا لِتَعْظِيمِ أُجُورِهِمْ أَوْ لِلتَّشْرِيعِ أَوْ لِلتَّسَلِّي عَنِ الدُّنْيَا، أَوْ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ لِخِسَّةِ قَدْرِهَا عِنْدَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى، وَعَدَمِ رِضَاهُ بِهَا دَارَ جَزَاءٍ لِأَنْبِيَائِهِ وَأَوْلِيَائِهِ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَحْوَالِهِمْ فِيهَا عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ.
And as for the messengers, may blessings and peace be upon them, what is necessary for them are truthfulness, trustworthiness, and delivery of what they were commanded to deliver to creation. And what is impossible for them, may blessings and peace be upon them, are the opposites of these attributes, which are lying, treachery by doing something they were forbidden with prohibition of prohibition or dislike, or concealing something they were commanded to deliver to creation. And what is permissible for them, may blessings and peace be upon them, are those human accidents that do not lead to deficiency in their lofty ranks, such as illness and the like. As for the proof for the necessity of their truthfulness, may blessings and peace be upon them, it is because if they were not truthful, lying would necessarily follow in the report of Him, exalted is He, due to His, exalted is He, confirming them by the miracle which stands in the place of His statement: 'My servant has spoken truthfully in all that he conveys from Me.' And as for the proof for the necessity of trustworthiness for them, may blessings and peace be upon them, it is because if they betrayed by doing a forbidden act or a disliked act, the forbidden or disliked would become obedience with respect to them, because God, exalted is He, commanded us to follow them in their sayings and actions, and God, exalted is He, does not command doing a forbidden or disliked act. And this very thing is the proof for the necessity of the third [attribute]. And as for the evidence for the permissibility of human accidents for them, it is the observation of their occurrence from them, either to magnify their rewards, or for legislation, or for consolation from this world, or to alert to the baseness of its worth with God, exalted is He, and His lack of satisfaction with it as a reward abode for His prophets and saints, considering their states in it, may blessings and peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage classifies prophetic attributes into three categories: necessary (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and permissible (ja'iz). It first lists the necessary attributes: truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amana), and delivery (tabligh) of the message. Their opposites—lying, treachery, and concealment—are impossible. Permissible for prophets are human accidents like illness that do not detract from their lofty rank. 

The proof for truthfulness is that God confirms prophets by miracles, which function as divine testimony 'My servant has spoken truthfully.' If they lied, God would be confirming a lie, which is impossible. The proof for trustworthiness is that God commands us to follow them. If they committed forbidden or disliked acts, then those acts would become acts of obedience by virtue of following them, but God does not command such acts. The same reasoning applies to delivery (the third attribute). Finally, the permissibility of human accidents is proven by observed occurrences in prophets' lives, which serve various purposes like reward, legislation, or teaching the lowliness of worldly life.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets cannot experience any human ailments or weaknesses.
Correction: Sanusi states that human accidents like illness are permissible for prophets and do not reduce their high rank. They occur for divine reasons such as increasing reward or teaching lessons.

Checkpoint
What is the proof for the necessity of truthfulness in prophets?
A) They are naturally honest.
B) If they lied, God would be confirming a lie through miracles.
C) They are required by law.
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The three categories of prophetic attributes and their rational proofs establish that prophets are truthful, trustworthy, and sinless in conveying revelation, while their human experiences do not imply any moral deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the Prophet's truthfulness is proven by miracles. How does that fact change the way you consider every hadith you read? If the Prophet could not lie, can you treat his words casually, or does this demand a decisive change in how you obey his instructions starting today?
?
The lesson proves that prophets never commit any sin, major or minor, because God commands you to follow them. Look at your own life: where are you knowingly following a habit that contradicts the Prophet's example? Are you willing to abandon that habit today, knowing that any sin you commit was impossible for him?
?
Prophets endured illnesses and difficulties for greater reward. When you next face a trial or sickness, will you complain or seize it as an opportunity to draw closer to God, following the example of the prophets? What specific intention can you set now to turn your next difficulty into worship?
